CURRENCY EQUIVALENTS
The Bangladesh Taka is officially valued at 30 to the Pound
Sterling. The Pound now floats relative to the US Dollar and conse-
quently the Taka, US Dollar rate is subject to change. The rate below
has been used throughout this report, except where stated to the con-
trary.
US$1                 =    Tk 13.0
Tk 1                 =    US$0.077
Tk 1 million         =    US$77.000

1 bigha                    -    0.33 ac.
1 mile (mi)                =    1.609 kilometers (km)
1 square mile (sq mi)    =    640 ac (259 ha)
1 foot (ft)                =    30.5 centimeters (cm)
1 maund (md)               =    82.3 lbs (37.3 kg)
1 metric ton (ton)         =    26.8 md
1 cubic foot per
second (cusec)       =    0.0283 cu meters per second

GLOSSARY
Aman           -    Rice planted before or during the monsoon and harvested
in November or December (B. aman is broadcast, T. aman
is transplanted)
Aus            -    Rice planted during March and April and harvested during
July and August (B. aus is broadcast, T. aus is
transplanted)
Bils           -    Depressions
Boro           -    Rice planted in winter and harvested during April to June
Char           -    Low-lying sandy land
Comilla
Cooperatives -    A system of two-tier cooperatives (at village and thana
levels) developed at the Bangladesh Academy for Rural
Development (Comilla) in the 1960s. The system emphasized
increasing the productivity of the small farmers through
training and by increasing their access to farm inputs
such as irrigation, credit and fertilizers.
District       -    Administrative unit in Bangladesh.  There are 19 Districts
in the country.
Katcha         -    Temporary (dirt) road
Khals          -    Natural channels
Kharif         -    Summer season
Mouja          -    Originally a cadastral map (now a map in which relative
locations can be found)
Paddy          -    Unhulled rice
Pucca           -    Permanent (paved) road
Rabi           -    Dry season
Taccavi        -    A relief loan to victims of natural catastrophies
Thana          -    Administrative unit in Bangladesh.  There are
413 thanas in the country.
Union          -    A unit of local self government.  There are about ten
unions in each thana
Ward           -    A subdivision of a union
FISCAL YEAR
July 1 to June 30

BANGLADESH
APPRAISAL OF RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        The Government of the People's Republic of Bangladesh has requested
IDA assistance to carry out a Rural Development Project in seven thanas of
the Bogra and Mymensingh Districts. The project consists of a rural works
program (rural roads, drains, rural markets, fish ponds), minor irrigation
(low-lift pumps, shallow tubewells, hand pumps) and thana facilities (office
and living quarters of thana officers, cooperative godowns). The project
also would provide for strengthening rural institutions and services (Comilla-
type cooperatives, as well as extension, animal health services, fisheries
and credit facilities).
ii.       The Government rural development objectives are to achieve food
self-sufficiency and create employment for the rural poor. Its strategy
uses the thanas as the focal point of development activities and emphasizes
labor-intensive, production oriented investments in minor irrigation and
rural infrastructure, improvements in agricultural supporting services and
setting-up of Comilla type cooperatives (now called TCCA-KSSs) for promoting
farmers' participation in development activities.
iii.      The proposed project would be the Bank Group's first rural develop-
ment project in Bangladesh. It is designed to strengthen and improve existing
rural institutions; to rectify domination of farm inputs and credit by the
more prosperous farmers; to promote more rapid expansion of high yielding
rice varieties of rainfed aus and transplanted aman, to increase the irrigated
boro area, and to create employment opportunities by reactivating the Rural
Works Program.
iv.       Total project cost is estimated at US$24.7 million equivalent of
which US$7.7 million, or about 31Z, would be in foreign exchange.  The pro-
posed IDA credit of US$16 million would finance the full foreign exchange
costs and some local currency requirements, or about 69% of the total proj-
ect cost net of taxes and duties (US$23.1 million). Equipment and office
supplies, estimated to cost US$4.6 million (net of contingencies) would be
procured through international competitive bidding in accordance with the
Bank Group guidelines. Civil works amounting to US$4.7 million (net of
contingencies) would be unsuitable for international competitive bidding
and would be carried out on the basis of local competitive bidding under
Government procedures which are satisfactory to IDA except for the earth works
which would be by force account. Rural credit (US$8.6 million) would be
administered by the Sonali Bank. Two revolving funds -- one for rural credit
and one for all other components -- would be established to ensure prompt
receipt of the appropriated funds by the various participating agencies.
v.        Local consultants would assist in the implementation of the rural
works and thana facilities programs. Short-term foreign consultants would
train TCCA/KSS staff in rural credit operations. Experts from several



- ii -
international/bilateral projects, currently operating in Bangladesh, would
provide additional technical assistance. Fellowships would be provided for
short-term training abroad of key Government staff.
vi.       Project implementation would require five years.  It will call for
effective coordination between a number of Government departments and agen-
cies.  To achieve this, project Executive Committees would be established
at Central, District and Thana levels, composed of officers from partici-
pating agencies. The Executive Committees would be chaired by the Director
General of the Integrated Rural Development Program at the Central level,
the Deputy Commissioner at the District, and the Circle Officer (Development)
at the Thana level. Except for comm>n items such as vehicles and office
equipment, each participating agency would do its own procurement. The
Central Executive Committee would collate and certify applications for re-
imbursement from IDA. A central Project Coordination Committee under the
co-chairmanship of the Secretary of Agriculture and the Secretary of Local
Government, Rural Development and Cooperatives, would attend to policy mat-
ters and would ensure timely approprLation of Government funds.
vii.      At full development by 1985, annual rice production from the
seven thanas as a result of the project is expected to reach 429,000 tons
compared to the current 247,000 tons and a projected 354,000 tons under
"without project" conditions. The project related increase of 75,000 tons
would represent an annual net foreigna exchange savings of about US$15.0
million.
viii.     The project would directly benefit about 150,000 farm families,
representing about 900,000 people or about half of the total population in
the project area. It is expected that most of the would-be beneficiaries are
small farmers with incomes below the subsistence level. During implementation,
the project would create the equivalent of about 12,000 man-years of employ-
ment for the landless people. At fuLl development, the project would also
increase farm labor requirements by .about 11 million man-days over the
current levels. Indirectly, the project would benefit a larger number of
people through improvements in rural roads, markets, animal health, pond
fishery and drains. Average annual per capita incomes of the project area
rural population would increase from about US$70 at present (compared to the
national average of US$100) to US$90 (compared to the projected national
average of US$110 in 1985. Based on increased paddy production alone, the
economic rate of return of the proje,:t is estimated to be over 40%. The
sensitivity analysis tests a number of adverse assumptions, and indicates
that the risks of the rates of retura falling below 30% are small.
ix.       The project would pioneer l replicable pattern of priority rural
development activities. It would involve an investment of about US$100
(excluding short-term credit and price contingencies) per rural family
spread over a five year period. Extension of the project to the estimated
10 million rural families in Bangladesh would represent a cost of about



- iii -
US$1 billion -- a substantial sum, but which would be within the means of
the country -- provided rural development receives the priority it deserves
in GOB's development efforts.
x.        The proposed project is suitable for an IDA credit of US$16 million
on standard IDA terms. The Borrower would be the People's Republic of
Bangladesh.






BANGLADESH
APPRAISAL OF RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of the People's Republic of Bangladesh (GOB) has
requested IDA assistance for US$16 million to finance a rural development
project in seven thanas in the Bogra and Mymensingh Districts. The project
seeks to evolve a model of rural development for Bangladesh, emphasizing:
(a) initiating a program of low cost, short gestation, production
oriented investments aimed at increasing agricultural pro-
duction;
(b) building and strengthening rural institutions to improve
agricultural supporting services and to ensure an equitable
supply of scarce agricultural inputs to the small farmers; and
(c) creating employment for rural landless people.
1.02      The project was designed and would be carried out in coordination
with the IDA-financed Agricultural and Rural Training project (Credit No.
621-BD). The components in the social sector which are not directly pro-
duction oriented, e.g. education, housing, and health are not included. The
successful implementation of the proposed project should, however, establish
a sound basis for such social infrastructure investments. Rural industries
have also been left out from this project, but they could be incorporated in
future investments in the project area.
1.03      The project is in line with the strategy set forth in the Bank
Group Land and Water Resources Sector Study (Report No. PS-13 dated December
1972). It was identified by the FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program (CP) and pre-
pared by an Interdepartmental Government Unit for Rural Development (IGU/RD),
supported by several CP missions. This appraisal report is based on the
findings of a mission comprising: Messrs. H.T. Chang; P.C. Garg; T.M. Kulatilaka
(IDA), and R.J. Shukle and W.J. Maddock (Consultants) who visited Bangladesh in
November/December 1974. A follow-up mission comprising Messrs. H.T. Chang,
T.M. Kulatilaka (IDA), and R.J. Shukle and Henri Vieilhescaze (Consultants)
visited Bangladesh again in February/March 1975.
II. BACKGROUND
General
2.01      Bangladesh, with a gross area of about 55,000 sq mi and an estimated
population of about 77 million, is among the most densely populated countries
in the world. Due to this as well as meager natural resources, poor physical



-2-
infrastructure and frequent natural calamities, much of the population lives
at or below absolute poverty levels. The current annual per capita income
of about US$100 (in 1975 prices) is among the lowest in the world.
2.02      The Government's First Five Year Plan (1973/74 - 1977/78), published
in October 1973, envisaged an ambitious goal of 8.8% per annum average growth
(over the 1972/73 level).  Taking into account the deteriorating terms of
trade since then and given the current situation, a growth of 3% per annum,
barely enough to keep pace with the population growth, would be a difficult task.
Agriculture
2.03      Bangladesh is predominantly agricultural.  The agriculture sector
accounts for as much as 55% of GNP, 75% of all employment and over 80Z of
all exports. It has, however, been unable either to adequately feed the
country's rapidly increasing populatLon or to provide gainful employment
to its largely unskilled and illiterate labor force.
2.04      Practically all 22.5 millian ac of cultivable land are already
under cultivation. The total cropped area in 1973/74 was about 30.5 mil-
lion ac, giving a cropping intensity of some 136%.  Of the cropped area,
rice accounts for 24.4 million ac; j7Lte 2.0 million ac and other crops 4.1
million ac.  Rice production in the 1960s increased at an annual average
rate of 2%, but lagged behind the population growth of 2.8% per year.  The
present level of annual food grain imports is about 2.5 million tons, or
about 20% of total consumption. The food imports have caused a heavy
strain on the foreign exchange reserves and have severely limited imports
of other essential commodities.
2.05      Agricultural development in Bangladesh is constrained by the
following:
(a) risk of floods - over 2.5 raillion ac are flooded up to
15 ft annually and cultivation cannot begin until after
the flood subsides in Novemgber. On 5.5 million ac,
annually flooded from 3-15 ft, only one crop per year
of low-yielding long stalked or floating rice is possible;
(b) lack of irrigation facilities - only 2.5 million ac are
irrigated during the November-March dry season;
(c) erratic weather conditions - cold spells in November-December
can seriously reduce the aman yield, while erratic monsoons
often cause droughts; and
(d) deficient transportation - transportation for moving farm
inputs and produce is inadequate and expensive.
2.06      Perhaps even more restrictive are the sociological and institutional
constraints. Some 30% of the village dwellers are landless. Among farmers
who own land, about 50% own 2.5 ac or less. Generally, small farmers in



Bangladesh are hard working and receptive to proven improved farming prac-
tices. But, their efforts have not been supplemented by an adequate and
timely supply of farm inputs, credit, and technical services. Existing
rural institutions, mostly staffed by poorly motivated and trained personnel,
and often dominated by larger farmers, have generally failed to fulfill these
key functions.
Rural Development Agencies and Programs
2.07      A number of Government ministries, institutions, departments and
autonomous agencies are responsible for providing supporting services to
rural areas. The agricultural extension service shortcomings were assessed
in connection with the Agricultural and Rural Training Project (Credit No.
621-BD, Report No. 680b-BD); fisheries and livestock services have lacked
active field programs. Distribution of improved seeds, fertilizers and
pesticides have favored the larger farmers. Impressive early gains under
the Thana Irrigation Program (TIP) have not been sustained due to poor
utilization of pumps and tubewells resulting in reduced area irrigated per
pump. The Rural Works Program (RWP), which constructed many rural roads in
the 1960s, has now lost its vitality due to poor project planning and im-
plementation constraints.
2.08      The traditional rural cooperatives in Bangladesh are now largely
defunct. The Bangladesh Cooperative Bank (BJSB) had 68% of its loans in arrears
in 1974.  Many of the larger farmers and Management Committee members of the
cooperatives are themselves defaulters. The new cooperative system initiated
in Comilla District in 1959 (para 4.08) introduced a sound approach as well
as adequate management skills for cooperative activity. After 1970, the
Government extended the two-tier Comilla cooperative system (Thana Central
Cooperative Associations -- TCCAs -- and Village Cooperative Societies -- KSSs),
to 154 thanas. But the overly rapid expansion and the generally depressed con-
ditions in recent years overtaxed the system. By mid-1972, the TCCA's also
had 41% of their loans overdue. In response, the Government changed its bank
for financing short-term credit for TCCAs from the Bangladesh Cooperative
Bank (BJSB) to the Sonali Bank, the largest nationalized commercial bank in
Bangladesh. Following this, the recovery of credit by TCCAs significantly
improved. The Government in its First Five Year Plan (1973-78) has placed
the emphasis of rural development on the strengthening of the TCCA-KSS Co-
operative System. Despite its shortcomings, the system is generally accepted
as a rural organization that, given suitable assistance and guidance, can be
made a viable system through which agricultural input, credit and services
can reach small farmers.
2.09      The problems of various existing rural programs are due to four
main causes:
(a) poor information on which to base equitable inputs
and credit distribution;
(b) lack of clearly defined working procedures for implementing
the various programs;



(c)  frequent sidestepping of approved procedures at
District and thana levels because of political
pressures; and
(d)  ineffective supporting seIvices due to insuffi-
ciently motivated, trainec:, organized and equipped
field staff.
2.10      As a result, the Government's development resources have gone
largely to the more prosperous farmers. These literate farmers largely
form the Cooperatives Management Conmittees, Ward Committees and Union
Councils membership. They are thus in a unique position to submit re-
quests for inputs and credit in the name of a cooperative, a village, a
ward or a union and to exert pressure on different levels of Government
officials to direct such supplies in their favor.
Project Formulation
2.11      The project has been formulated to remove or minimize the con-
straints mentioned above. Improved landownership registers and maps would
be prepared to promote equitable allocation of farm inputs. Inter-agency
coordination would be improved by establishing clear work procedures and
by delineating the areas of responsilbility for the various participating
agencies. The supporting services would be strengthened by staff training,
by increasing staff mobility and by Lmproving the work environment for the
field staff. Attempts would also be made to shield the field staff from
local political pressures by dealing with those pressures at the higher
administrative levels. The joining of TCCAs by small farmers would be made
attractive, but strict credit discipline would be enforced. Specific pro-
ject components are described in detail in Chapter IV and the Annexes.
2.12      The project covers only a :Limited area - its main objective is
to serve as a model for revitalizing rural development programs. Replica-
tion of this project is expected to follow soon through projects financed
by the Government from its own resources and with external assistance.
III. THE PRLOJECT AREA
General
3.01      Bangladesh has 19 Districts, 413 thanas and about 4,500 unions.
The project would cover seven thanas: four (Kotwali, Sherpur, Gabtali, and
Sariakandi) in Bogra District and three (Muktagacha, Trisal, and Gaffar-
gaon) in Mymensingh District (Map 11599). The four Bogra thanas are about
90 mi northwest of Dacca, and the three Mymensingh thanas are about 55 mi
north of Dacca. The seven thanas consist of 90 unions with a total gross



- 5 -
area of about 612,000 ac. The estimated 1974 population for the seven thanas
was about 1.70 million; giving a population density of about 1,700 persons/
sq     mi compared to about 1,400 persons/sq mi nationally.  The project
thanas have land and water features representative of the country as a whole.
Climate
3.02      The project area has a warm monsoon climate with three main seasons:
the wet monsoon season, June - October; the dry season, November - February,
and the pre-monsoon season, March - May. Even during the wet season, rain-
fall is erratic.
3.03      The mean annual rainfall is about 68 inches for the Bogra thanas
and about 88 inches for the Mymensingh thanas; 80% falls during the monsoon.
Temperatures are in the mean monthly range of 70-85°F. The daily minimum
temperature may drop to below 60°F in late November and the daily maximum
may rise to 95°F in April and May. Relative humidity is generally high
(Annex 1).
Topography
3.04      The four Bogra thanas are bounded on the east by the Jamuna reach
of the Brahmaputra River. The Bengali River and its tributary the Karatoa
River intersect the area (Map 11599).  West of the Karatoa River, the land
rises to an elevated terrace of Barind Tract soils. Land to the east is
mostly level with old river meanders, silty ridges and numerous perennially
flooded depressions. The land slopes generally to the southwest between 60
and 40 ft above mean sea level (MSL).
3.05      The three Mymensingh thanas are situated between the Banar River
and the Old Brahmaputra River course (Map 11599), and are traversed by the
Shirkali-Muhuri and Sutia rivers. The lands are generally level but con-
tain numerous depressions, ridges and old meanders. Elevation varies from
45 ft to 40 ft above MSL in Muktagacha thana and from 35 ft to 20 ft (MSL)
in Gaffargaon and Trisal thanas.   The southwestern part of Muktagatcha
and the southern third of Gaffargaon form a slightly elevated area - Madhupur
Tract - intersected by creeks draining to the Banar River.
3.06      The seven project thanas are presently drained only by natural
watercourses, which have been extensively eroded, silted and obstructed.
Intense monsoon rains cause ponding 6 ft or deeper over about 10% of the
area, 3-6 ft deep over about 12%, 1-3 ft 41% and 0-1 ft over 37% (Annex 1).
Soils
3.07      Except for the Barind Tract in Bogra thanas and the Madhupur
Tract in Mymensingh thanas, the soils are relatively recent deltaic
alluvia. Because of the numerous streams flowing through the delta,
soils are deep, of complex clay, and have silt and sand stratifications.



-6-
The Barind and Madhupur tracts are relatively old alluvia of red clay.
The project area has moderate-to-good agricultural land capable of sus-
taining the whole range of crops growon in Bangladesh. The present land-
use pattern is largely determined by land elevation and soil moisture
retention characteristics (Annex 1).
Water Resources
3.08      The surface water resources available for dry season pump irriga-
tion in the project are limited. In the four Bogra thanas, the use of water
from the big Jamuna River is presently restricted by the Brahmaputra flood
embankment. The Bengali River's smaLl dry season flow must be largely reserved
for maintaining dry season water transport. The Karatoa River is non-peren-
nial.  In the three Mymensingh thanas, the Old Brahmaputra River course is
virtually dry in the winter. The lower reaches of the Kaoraid and Banar
river systems are subject to a small fresh water tidal inflow during the
dry season. Annual inundation fills the bils and natural drains and pro-
vides a small amount of stored water for dry season use.
3.09      Recent experience in the Bogra thanas indicates that the limit for
low-lift pump use is now being reached. In the Mymensingh thanas, a small
increase in number of pumps utilizing water from existing depressions would
be possible.
3.10      Although substantial groundwater resources for shallow and deep
tubewells exist throughout the projezt area, only limited tubewell exploita-
tion has been undertaken (Annex 1).
Agricultural Production
3.11      The present cropped area in the seven thanas is about 745,000 ac
which, on a net cultivable area of 457,000 ac, gives an overall cropping
intensity of about 160%, as compared with the national average of 136%.
The cropping intensity varies from 139% in Sherpur thana to 176% in
Muktagacha thana.
3.12      Paddy accounts for about 83% of the cropped area.  There are four
seasonal paddy crops: aus, broadcast aman (B. aman), transplanted aman (T.
aman), and boro. The seasonality and relative risks of failures of these
four crops are discussed in Annex 2. Double paddy cropping is practiced at
present on some land. Aus is grown on 195,000 ac, broadcast aman on 34,000
ac, transplanted aman on 352,000 ac and boro on 39,000 ac. Local variety
paddy predominates, with only 14% of high yielding varieties (HYV). The
local varieties yield only an average of (tons/ac): aus and B. aman- 0.5;
T. aman - 0.6, and boro - 0.7. The '4YV aus, T. aman and boro paddy yield
about 0.8, 0.9 and 1.2 tons per ac, respectively (Annex 2).



- 7 -
3.13      Jute with an average yield of about 0.5 t/ac accounts for 72,000
ac (10% of the cropped area). About 53,000 ac of dry season pulses, oil-
seeds, chillies, potatoes and other vegetables are grown utilizing residual
soil moisture and minor supplemental irrigation.
3.14      Agricultural production in the area in 1974 was estimated at
about 380,000 tons of paddy and 35,000 tons of Jute. Production data of
minor crops, such as pulses, oilseeds, vegetables and potatoes are incom-
plete.
3.15      Farmers have accepted the use of chemical fertilizers for HYV
varieties. Fertilizers, however, have largely gone to the larger farmers,
particularly when they were in short supply (i.e. 1974/75 boro crop and
1975 aman crop) which made rationing necessary. Most small farmers who
could not get their names on the sanctioned lists failed to receive fertilizers
at the low official price. Fertilizers are no longer rationed at present.
Farm work is carried out by an estimated 265,000 draft animals and by manual
labor. Animal health is poor and severely restricts farm draft power.
Diseases cause an annual death rate of 8% and keep about 20% of the animals
unfit for work. About 70% are infested with parasites (para 4.08 (c)).
3.16      A large number of ponds in the project thanas can produce fish
but most Government and communal ponds are unattended, dilapidated and non-
productive. Privately owned ponds produce an average of about 250 lb of
fish per ac of fish pond per year.
Transportation
3.17      The three Mymensingh thanas are connected to Dacca by a paved road
and a railroad. The four Bogra thanas are also connected to Dacca by a paved
road, but ferry crossings at several points cause long delays. A railroad
line passes through Bogra, Kotwali and Gabtali thanas but it is not connected
to Dacca. There is also no direct road or rail link between the two project
Districts, nor is there an air link. Thana headquarters are connected to
District headquarters by poorly maintained paved roads. A network of earth
roads link the rural hinterland to the markets and other transportation
arteries. Due to frequent floods and lack of maintenance, these roads are
in poor condition. In total, there are about 140 mi of paved roads and
about 1,000 mi of dirt roads in the project area. The Brahmaputra River and
other rivers are used throughout the year for boat transport of cargo and
passengers. Natural khals (channels) provide access to some villages by
small boats.
Storage, Marketing and Processing Facilities
3.18      Farm produce in the project thanas is largely for subsistence.
Less than 25% of the total paddy crop and a somewhat higher percentage of
vegetables, fruits and p ises are marketed. Jute is the only crop grown
primarily for the market. Farmers take their produce by carry-pole, oxcart



- 8 -
and boat to the village markets, where it is bought by small traders and
then sold to successively large brokers before reaching the wholesale deal-
ers in the town market. The rural markets congregate in open air. Frequent
monsoon rains disrupt the markets and often force small farmers to sell at
unfavorable prices. Small farmers usually sell soon after harvest. Tradi-
tionally, the lowest paddy price is received during November-January for the
main aman crop.
3.19      Farmers usually store paddy in containers of bamboo strips and
clay, placed inside their houses. The containers are only satisfactory if
paddy is well dried. Many of the private dealers have small godowns for
the storage of paddy and rice. Substantial storage losses, particularly
of the monsoon harvested aus and borD paddy are suspected. Rice for home
consumption is processed by hand pounding and by rice huskers operated by
village entrepreneurs. Most marketed paddy is milled in some 40 commercial
rice mills. There are also 12 privately operated oilseed mills, 18 jute-
balers and cold storage facilities for potatoes in the Bogra thanas.
3.20      Prior to 1974, the Government did not intervene in the market.
Jute purchases by Government-owned mLlls have been at the secondary market
level. However, after the 1974 floods when smuggling and hoarding raised
the paddy price to Tk 200/md (US$650/ton at the then prevailing exchange
rate), GOB imposed compulsory paddy procurement at Tk 74/md. This policy
of procurement was suspended in 1975.
Population, Farm Size and Land Tenure
3.21      The 1974 census estimated t:he project area population at about
1.70 million of which about 0.25 mil ion are primarily non-agricultural.
A socio-economic survey conducted by IGU/RD during project preparation
indicated an average of 5.8 members lper rural household. On this basis,
it is estimated that there are about 250,000 agriculture-dependent house-
holds in the project thanas. The rural population lives in some 1,400
villages varying in population size f-rom about 500 to 2,500.
3.22      The average project area farm size is about 1.9 ac (from 1.0 ac
in Trisal to about 2.5 ac in Sherpur). Most farm holdings in the area are
heavily fragmented with plot sizes averaging about 0.3 ac. The IGU/RD
surveys showed:

3.23      Some 30% of the families are essentially landless and another 20%
own one acre or less. About 45% of the total cultivated land is owned by 10%
of the farm families with holdings larger than 5 ac. About 80% of the culti-
vated land is owner-operated. The remaining 20% is leased by small farmers
from absentee landlords or larger farmers on a share cropping basis. About
45% of the rural families are owner-operators, 25% owner-cum-share croppers,
5% landless share croppers and 25% landless rural workers. Share cropping
is usually based on a 50-50 sharing of the crop with cash inputs shared on
a variable basis between the landowner and the share cropper. There are no
laws in Bangladesh to protect the tenure of the share croppers.
3.24      On the average, small farmers have greater illiteracy rates,
smaller membership rates in cooperative groups, fewer livestock and lower
access to irrigation water and to institutional credit. These factors
combine to constrain small farmers' productivity and depress their income.
The advantage of the larger size of holdings is magnified by the privileged
position of the larger farmers in terms of access to credit and farm inputs.
IV. THE PROJECT
General
4.01      The project would strengthen a number of production oriented on-
going rural programs in the project thanas. To ensure that benefits reach
the smaller farmers, physical inputs and rural credit provided under the proj-
ect would be channeled through the Comilla type of cooperatives (TCCA-KSSs).
A major effort would be made to improve the operation and management of
these cooperatives. Technical services to the cooperative members would
be strengthened. The project would comprise rural works; minor irrigation
facilities; thana facilities; and rural credit. The project would also aim
at strengthening of rural institutions and services and would include tech-
nical assistance, and monitoring and evaluation. A brief description of
project works is given below.



- 10 -
Project Works
4.02      Rural Works (Annex 3) would rehabilitate 6 mi of paved roads
and construct 9 mi of new roads to connect Gabtali and Sariakandi thanas
to the Bogra District headquarters; improve 300 mi of earth dirt roads
(Maps 1600, 11601, 11615); rehabilitate 500 ac of abandoned fish ponds;
construct 40 paved rural markets; and improve 60 mi of drainage channels.
These works would generate about four million man-days of employment over
a five year period. Assurances have been obtained from GOB to follow the
procedures and timetables detailed in Annex 3, paras 17 and 19.
4.03      Minor irrigation (Annex 4) would replace about 805 existing oversized
two cusec low-lift pumps with about 1,400 one cusec pumps over three years;
provide about 2,500 new 1/2 cusec si]allow tubewells and about 5,000 hand
pumps. Low-lift pumps and shallow tubewell equipment would be distributed by
the Bangladesh Agricultural Development Corporation (BADC) to TCCA-KSS
members. Through better pump allocation procedures, the irrigation efficiency
of the low-lift pumps is expected to increase from 19 ac per cusec at present to
25 ac per cusec. The shallow tubewells would irrigate 15 ac per well. These
powered pumps and wells are expected to increase the irrigated rice area from
39,000 ac at present to about 97,000 ac. The hand pumps would each serve
about 0.3 to 1 ac. To help enforce the discipline proposed in this project
and to enable BADC to concentrate on technical services, assurances have been
obtained from GOB that in the seven thanas during project implementation, BADC
would not extend credit to the farmtrs in the project area for the purchase of
shallow tubewells. The Government has indicated that it will impose the same
restriction on BKB In the project area, primarily in order to help impgove
cooperative credit discipline. Assurances have also been obtained from GOB
for following the procedures and timetables detailed in Annex 4, paras 20-26
and for providing an adequate supply of fuel, oil and spares for pumps and
wells in the project thanas.
4.04      Thana Facilities (Annex 5) would complete the construction of four
Thana Training and Development Centers (TTDC) and four houses for the Thana
Circle Officers-Development (para. 5.02); construct 11 residential buildings
for thana officers, build four new fertilizer warehouses and seven new gen-
eral stores at the TCCA headquarters. Assurances have been obtained from
GOB for implementing the procedures and timetables detailed in Annex 5,
paras 11 to 15.
4.05      Rural Credit (Annex 6).  The project would provide the estimated
net incremental short-term credit requirements of about Tk 42 million and
would finance equipment worth about Tk 70 million (net of price contingencies)
through medium-term credit. The short-term credit would be for financing the
cost of cash inputs (mainly fertilizers and pesticides) used in crop produc-
tion. Medium-term credit would finance the purchase of shallow tubewells,
hand pumps, and simple processing equipment such as paddy driers and rice
husking machines. The credit would be channeled to the TCCA-KSS members
through the Sonali Bank, the largest nationalized Commercial Bank in the
country.



- 11 -
4.06      Short-term loans would be made for a period of six months (with
the exception of loans for sugarcane which would be for a period of 14 months).
Users of the medium-term loan would put up 10% of the total cost as downpayment
and repay the rest in up to five annual installments. Interest rate charges
for short- and medium-term loans at the farmers' level would be 17.5% and
13%, respectively. A part of the interest charges would be retained by the
Bangladesh Bank as a contribution to a credit Guarantee Fund and by TCCA-KSSs
as a provision for bad debts.
4.07      An inventory of outstanding institutional debts by farmers would be
made under the joint supervision of the Registrar of Cooperatives and the
Director General of the Integrated Rural Development Program (IRDP). To
help ensure credit discipline, Guidelines would be prepared and agreed to
with IDA, before credit effectiveness, for repayment or rescheduling of the
outstanding debts. To be eligible to join TCCA-KSSs, farmers would have to
settle (or reschedule) their past debts in a manner consistent with the
Guidelines. Debt inventory would be carried out in the first year of the
project for members of all cooperatives (UMPCS, TIP-KSS and TCCA-KSS) in the
project thanas. Since the TIP-KSS pump groups are to be reorganized into
TCCA-KSS pump groups, their members' debt would be repaid or rescheduled and
agreements for repayment signed with their debtors before their acceptance
to the TCCA-KSS membership. Those who fail to comply would forfeit their
right to belong to any pump group. The UMPCS in the project thanas would be
similarly reorganized into TCCA-KSSs. Farmers not belonging to any cooperative
and without outstanding cooperative debts would be recruited by TCCA-KSSs
outright. Loan processing, supervision and bookkeeping procedures of TCCA-KSSs
would be improved to minimize willful defaulting. Assurances have been obtained
from GOB to follow procedures detailed in Annex 6, paras 15 to 40.
4.08      Strengthening of Rural Institutions and Services would include
providing office, field, and laboratory equipment and vehicles for field
officers; improving working procedures; conducting short training courses
for departmental officers at the District, thana and union levels, and
for managers, accountants, model farmers and inspectors at the TCCA-KSS
level. The specific improvements planned for the various services are
summarized below:
(a) TCCA-KSS (Annex 7). The main purpose would be to strengthen
TCCA-KSS operation and to promote equitable input and credit
distribution through: revision of membership registration to
include assets (land and draft animals) and liabilities (out-
standing debts of members); improved screening of input and
rural credit application; better financial and accounting
procedures to make all applications based on verifiable data;
preparation of KSS land registers; coordination of extension,
livestock and fishery services; staff training, and expansion
of KSS membership throughout the area. Provision of low-lift
pumps, shallow tubewells, hand pumps, processing machines,



- 12 -
and fish ponds under the project exclusively to TCCA-KSS
members would facilitate expansion of TCCA-membership
coverage. The TCCA-KSS will receive scheduled visits by
extension livestock and ;-fishery officers and mass vaccination
and deworming of draft animals and poultry. The above
measures would enable matll farmers to know their rights
so that larger farmers can no longer monopolize goods,
services and credit. The GOB agreement to implement the
IRDP/TCCA/KSS work program as detailed in Annex 7, paras 17 to
34, and Exhibit 2 has been obtained.
(b) Agricultural Extension (Annex 8). The main tasks would be
to increase the effectiveness of the TCCA-KSS model farmer
training.  The training urould emphasize field practice in-
stead of classroom teaching. Extension officers would visit
TCCA-KSS villages on scheduled dates. The technical objectives
within ten years would stress improvement of basic cultural
practices in addition to use of inputs, to introduce the KSS
plan of land-use, and to extend HYV on 75% of land identified
as suitable for HYV T. anan and 50% for HYV aus. Special atten-
tion will also be paid to improvement of jute and vegetables.
Assurances have been obtained from GOB to intensify the agricul-
tural extension program as detailed in Annex 8, Exhibit 1.
(c) Livestock Improvement (Annex 9). The primary objective would
be to reduce prevailing high mortality and disability bf
draft animals by controlling major diseases and parasites.
Demonstrations of improved forage and fodder crops would also
be carried out. Assurances have been obtained frou GOB to
strengthen the livestock and veterinarian services as detailed
in Annex 9, paras 9 to 18.
(d) Pond Fisheries (Annex 10). The primary objectives would be
to bring into production the delerict ponds and to provide
an additional source of iacome for landless people in the vil-
lages. Some 500 ac of putlicly-owned fish ponds would be
selected by Thana Fishery Officers (TFO) and reclaimed by the
Rural Works Program (RWP) (Annex 3, para 16). Landless people
would be organized into TCCA Fishery Cooperatives, to manage
the reclaimed ponds. District Fishery Officers (DFO) would
conduct demonstration ponds, advise Cooperatives on pond
management and supply fislh fries. Assurances have been ob-
tained from GOB to increasie pond fish production as detailed
in Annex 10, paras 5 to 10.
(e) Training (Annex 18). The in-service training program essential
for strengthening rural institutions and services would be an
extension of the IDA Agricultural and Rural Training Project
(Credit No. 621-BD). The trainees would include the District



- 13 -
and thana IRDP, extension, livestock and fishery officers,
and Union Agricultural Assistants. These officers, in turn,
would train the TCCA-KSS managers, accountants, inspectors
and model farmers. New officers joining the project would
also be trained. Training costs are included under different
categories.
4.09      Technical Assistance (Annex 19).  The project would employ con-
sultants for assisting the Ministry of Local Government, Rural Development
and Cooperatives in implementing the rural works and thana facility programs,
as well as short-term consultants for providing training to TCCA/KSS staff
on rural credit operations and subsequent supervision. For other project
components, GOB has given assurances that it will make arrangements, as and
when necessary, with the following international and bilateral agencies for
their experts in Bangladesh to provide technical assistance to this project:
Agricultural Extension         --  UNDP/FAO Extension Project, UNDP/FAO
Senior Land-Use Advisor and IRRI
experts at BRRI
Livestock                     ---  German Livestock Team
Minor Irrigation               --  UNDP/ILO project for BADC
Rural Works Program            --  USAID/CARE
The project costs include a component for these expected additional expendi-
tures to be incurred for these services as well as for training GOB officers
overseas. The Central Project Executive Committee (para 5.03) would collate
requests of participating agencies for specific technical assistance.
4.10      Monitoring and Evaluation (Annex 11).  Project implementation
would involve a large number of agencies, and would require adherence to
strict schedules and working procedures. Also, because of its complexity
it is possible that some project components might not proceed as planned
and might need revision. A close supervision and monitoring would, accord-
ingly, be of crucial importance for coordinating the efforts of various
agencies and for spotting the problem areas at an early date. The monitoring
system for the project would be built into the regular progress reports by
the thana and District officers of the various agencies. Project Executive
Committees at the various levels (para 5.02) would compile and review the
reports and recommend appropriate actions. To avoid burdening the field
staff unnecessarily, the monitoring would be concentrated on data of key
importance to project progress. Also, the reporting format would be designed
so that the field staff are not asked to report on items which they cannot
measure/estimate with reasonable accuracy.
4.11      Due to the importance of this project to rural development in
Bangladesh, the impact of the various project components would also re-
quire an in-depth evaluation. Particular emphasis would be given on



- 14 -
assessing the impact of the project on small farmers and on rural land-
less. Evaluation would be conducted twice -- at the mid-point and at
the end of project implementation. IJpdated socio-economic data from the
1974 survey by IGU/RD, supplemented as necessary, would be the benchmark.
The evaluation would be conducted by an independent organization acceptable
to IDA. Assurances have been obtained from GOB that within six months of
credit effectiveness GOB would submit: to IDA, for concurrence, a detailed
plan for the evaluation.
4.12      The project would also require heavier than normal supervision
by IDA. Implementation schedules for key project activities, likely to
require close supervision during implementation, are shown in Annex 11,
Exhibit 1.
Cost Estimates
4.13      Total project costs are estimated at US$24.7 million equivalent,
including US$1.6 million in taxes and duties. The foreign exchange com-
ponent is estimated at US$7.7 million or 31% of the total. Estimates are
based on preliminary designs, with unit prices at July 1975 levels. An
amount of 10% has been added to these for engineering and administrative
costs for rural works, minor irrigation and thana facilities. A 20%
physical contingency is allowed for the rural works and 10% for all
other categories, except for low-lift pumps, shallow tubewells and hand
pumps, for which the numbers are fixed and unit prices are firm. Pro-
jected cost increases due to inflation have been calculated assuming the
following inflation rates:

Financing
4.14      The proposed IDA credit of US$16 million would finance the full
foreign exchange cost and about half of local currency costs or about 69% of
the total project costs, net of taxes and duties. The Government is respons-
ible for financing the costs not covered by IDA credit. Funds equivalent to
US$1.6 million would come from the Sonali Bank which in addition to its
present portfolio, would provide half of the incremental short-term credit
needs of the project area farmers. Users of medium-term credit would put up
US$0.8 million as down payment and would provide another US$2.3 million
as installment payments during the project implementation period. The
GOB would finance the remaining project costs of US$4.0 million through



- 16 -
annual budget appropriations . In view of the low foreign exchange reserves
of Bangladesh, the difficulties in financing local currency costs from bud-
getary resources, and the high priority nature of this project, the above
financing plan is considered appropriate.
4.15      A consolidated Project Evaluation Proforma (PEP) 1/ has been approved
by GOB for the project as a whole, with programs and budgets for different
agencies listed under separate headtiags. According to the project plan and
PEP, funds for defraying the project costs would be provided by GOB to
different agencies, as additional appropriation to their respective budgets.
4.16      Assurances have been obtained from GOB that the Government would
establish two revolving funds -- one for the rural credit component and one
for all other components -- and would replenish these funds at the beginning
of each month to maintain balances adequate to cover the estimated requirements
for the following three months. A condition of effectiveness would be that
GOB has made initial contributions of Ti 4.0 million each to the two funds
(in the case of the rural credit fund, Tk 2.0 million each to short- and
medium-term sub-accounts, respectively).
Procurement
4.17      Except for comon items such as vehicles and office equipment,
each agency would be responsible for its own procurement under the project
and would submit all documents to the Central Project Executive Committee
(para 4.21). The Comittee would be responsible for procurement of common
items, for maintaining records of all equipment and services procured under
the project, and for collating applications for reimbursement from IDA.
Equipment and supplies estimated to cost about US$4.4 million (net of con-
tingencies) would be procured after international competitive bidding in
accordance with IDA Guidelines. A preference limited to 15X of the cif price
of imported goods or the custom duty, whichever is lower, would be extended
to local manufacturers in the evaluation of bids.  Procurement of off-the-
shelf imported items costing less than US$10,000 each would be purchased
through normal Government procurement: procedures, which are satisfactory to
IDA. Such purchases would, however, be limited to US$200,000 in total.
Goods financed with short-term credit. would be procured through com ercial
chamels.
4.18      Vehicles estimated to cost US$200,000 would be procured through
local competitive bidding under Government procedures satisfactory to IDA,
since the number of vehicles involved (Annex 13, Table 2) is small and
therefore it would be difficult to attract foreign bidders for these con-
tracts.
1/   The PEP is a project report prepared for the Planning Commission.
Approval of the PEP by an interministerial National Economic Council
(NEC) is required before local funds for the project can be released.



- 17 -
4.19      Because most of the civil works amounting to US$4.7 million (net
of contingencies) would be simple, individually very small, labor-intensive,
scattered over a wide area, restricted to seasonal construction, and could
not be grouped into large contracts, they are unsuitable for international
competitive bidding. With the exception of the earth works, they would be
carried out on the basis of local competitive bidding under Government pro-
cedures satisfactory to IDA. The earth works of RWP and thana facilities,
would be carried out on a force account basis, as they are customarily done
under the supervision of the Union Committee for Rural Works Program.
Implementation Schedule
4.20      Project implementation would take five (six fiscal) years.  All oper-
ational equipment would be procured in the first year. The construction of
project buildings would be completed in two years; the construction of paved
roads and replacement of low-lift pumps in three years. Other project works
would be spread over five years in line with the growth of TCCA-KSSs (Annex
11, Exhibit 1).
Disbursements
4.21      Disbursements from the credit account would be made for (a) 70% of
the cost of civil works; (b) 100% of foreign expenditures or 100% of local
expenditures ex-factory or 70% of other local expenditures for vehicles and
equipment (Annex 13, Table 2); (c) 100% of foreign expenditures and 70% of
local expenditure for consulting services, technical assistance and project
evaluation; and (d) 50% of local expenditures for incremental short-term
rural credit requirements. 1/ Disbursements against (a) and (b) and individual
local expenditure costing more than US$1,000 equivalent would be fully
documented. Disbursements against (d) and individual local expenditures
costing less than US$1,000 under (c) would be made against certificate of
expenditure the documentation for which would not be submitted but would be
available for review during the course of project supervision. It is ex-
pected that disbursements would be completed by December 31, 1981 (assuming
credit becomes effective before July 1, 1976), approximately six months after
the end of a five year implementation period. An estimated schedule of ex-
penditures of the project, a semi-annual disbursement schedule and the pro-
posed allocation of the proceeds of the credit are shown in Annex 13. The
Central Project Executive Committee (para 5.03) would be responsible for
collating all project accounts of all participating agencies and for preparing
and sending certified applications for reimbursement from IDA.
1/   Incremental requirements refer to increment of lending at any time over
that of the highest lending volume previously. For Year One, it would
be the increment over the 1975 T. aman loans made by the Sonali Bank
in the project thanas (Annex 6, para 24).



- 1B -
Accounts and Audit
4.22      All agencies participating in project implementation are Government
bodies subject to normal Government control and auditing procedures. Assur-
ances have been obtained from GOB that:
(a) All participating agencieni would maintain separate
accounts for the project a1t thana, District and
Central levels and would *,end copies to the
respective Project Execut.ive Committees.
(b) Auditors acceptable to IDiA would audit these
accounts annually and the audited accounts,
together with the auditor's comments, would
be sent to IDA within six months of the close
of each fiscal year.
(c) Auditing of the rural cre<lit accounts would continue
until all loans provided to farmers during the project
implementation period are repaid.
Environmental Impact
4.23      The project would enhance the environment by improving land drain-
age, rehabilitating stagnant fish ponds, providing water supplies to rural
markets and road access for rural health services. The extension service
would teach farmers the safe use of pesticides.
V. ORGANIZAI'ION AND MANAGEMENT
General
5.01      The project, aimed at institution-building, would utilize the
existing GOB organizations at all levels. It would focus on creating a
replicable pattern of interagency coordination at the field level by es-
tablishing clear work procedures and by delineating the area of responsi-
bility for the various participating agencies. Also, by providing improved
accommodations (office and residential), equipment and vehicles and job-
related training, the project would enhance staff motivation as well as
effectiveness. The work procedures and agency responsibilities are given
in Annexes 3 through 10. Detailed project organization charts are given
in Annex 12.
Project Executive Committees (Annex 12)
5.02      The Project Executive Committees have already been established.
There are seven thana committees, two District committees and a Central
committee. Circle Officers (Development) serve as the thana committee



- 19 -
chairmen and Deputy Commissioners, as chairmen of the District committees.
The Director General of IRDP serves as the chairman of the Central Project
Executive Committee. The IRDP officers serve as executive secretaries of
the committees at all three levels. All participating agencies are re-
presented on the three levels of committees, thus maintaining their re-
spective chains of command.
5.03      The Central Project Executive Committee would be responsible for
overall project execution and coordination, and for reporting progress to
and applying for reimbursement from IDA. The Committee would review the
annual work programs of the various participating agencies. It would also
manage the two revolving funds to be established for the project. The IRDP
Director for Extension and Training would serve as the secretary of the
committee.
Central Project Coordination Committee
5.04      A Central Project Coordination Committee has been formed under the
co-chairmanship of the Secretaries of Agriculture/Local Government, Rural
Development and Cooperatives. Committee members include: Chief, Rural
Institution Division, Planning Commission; Joint Secretary of Ministry of
Forestry, Fishery and Livestock; Chairman of BADC; Director, Agricultural
Credit Department of the Bangladesh. Bank, and Director General of IRDP who
would also serve as the Committee secretary. The Committee would determine
policy issues and approve detailed work procedures submitted by the Central
Project Executive Committee. Approved procedures would be issued as official
orders by ministries concerned. The Committee would see to it that Govern-
ment project funds will be allocated to the implementing agencies according
to tue PEP, and that major coordination problems are resolved. Also, it would
correspond with IDA on policy matters related to the project. Assurance
has been obtained that GOB would make lands available whenever such lands
are needed for implementing the project.
Agency Responsibilities
5.05      The Ministry of Local Government, Rural Development and Coopera-
tives has four agencies participating in the project areas:
(a)  IRDP - The IRDP is responsible for organizing and supervising
the operation and management of TCCA-KSS in the country.
It would be responsible for improvement of TCCA-RSS
operation as stipulated in Annex 7. In addition, it
would be responsible for the day-to-day coordination
of project progress at all levels (Annex 7).

construction and rehabilitation of dirt roads and
rural markets, while the Union Committees for RWP

KSSs; one AI for every 12 KSSs, and one Inspector for every
five AI. It is projected that the VA would be increased
from 104 at present to 167 by Year Five; AI from 52 to 83,
and Inspectors from 10 to 17. The salaries and other ex-
penses for the employment of these officers are expected to
be met increasingly from the TCCAs' increased incomes from
the rural credit and other service commissions during the

that the VE&s to be assigned to the project area would be solely engaged

in agricultural extension activities. The issuance of the detailed

Operation and Maintenance
5.11      Operation and Maintenance (O & M) cost for equipment and vehicles
for the various participating agencies would be provided by the project
during the five year implementation period. After project implementation,
GOB would provide adequate 0 & M costs through its regular budgetary appro-
priations to the concerned agencies. The 0 & M cost of low-lift pumps,
shallow tubewells, processing machines, fish ponds, and rural markets
would be borne by the users. The maintenance cost of thana facilities,
roads, and drains would be borne by GOB.
Charges
5.12      The BADC-owned low-lift pumps are rented to user groups at a
country-wide rental of Tk 600 per 2 cusec pump per year (dry season use
only) and Tk 350 per year per 1 cusec pump. In addition, the pump group
pays for diesel oil, spares and the pump driver. These charges cover
only about 50% of the overall cost of pump irrigation. They do repre-
sent, however, a substantial improvement over the previous years when
BADC used to pay for the spares and the driver as well. The GOB's first
Five Year Plan aims at eliminating all subsidies to irrigation by the
end of the plan period. The project would adopt the national rates.
Shallow tubewells and hand pumps to be provided under the project would
be sold to the users at full cost.
5.13      Currently, vaccination of animals and poultry is provided free of
cost. Partially, this policy reflects the ineffectiveness of the current
programs. To promote a rapid acceptance of the mass vaccination and de-
wormiag programs proposed under the project, the existing policy would have
to be followed until the project has produced a visible impact at the village
level. Assurances have been obtained that within three years of the credit
effectiveness, GOB would review the situation and propose a fee collection
plan from the users to recover at least the 0 & M costs of the equipment and
vehicles in use for the livestock disease control program.
5.14      Assurances have also been obtained that within one year of the
credit effectiveness GOB would present to IDA, for concurrence, a proposal
for the management of the rural marksets along with a schedule of fees/rents
to be collected from the users.

VI. PRODUCTION, MARKETING, PRICES AND FARM INCOMES
6.01      Improved extension and land-use planning services, better farmer
organization, more equitable input and rural credit distribution, improved
drainage and increase in irrigated area would lead to a higher cropping
intensity (rising from about 160% to 1742), accelerated transformation into
HYV's, and increased yields. It is estimated that at full development in
Year Ten, HYV's would extend to about 75% of the potential areas 1/ suitable
for HYV T. aman cultivation under rainfed conditions and to 502 for HYV
aus.
6.02      Paddy would continue to be the dominant crop; the most significant
developments would be the accelerated replacement of traditional rice varie-
ties by HYV's and improvements in yields as indicated below:

Project area paddy production would increase from about 380,000 tons at
present to about 660,000 tons at full development in Year Ten; in the
absence of the project, however, production is estimated to rise to about
545,000 tons (Annex 2).
6.03      Jute yields would increase slightly, but the area planted is
expected to remain static. Rabi production of potatoes, vegetables and
oil seeds would increase substantially, but are not accounted for in the
project analysis due to unpredictable variations in annual planted acreage.
1/   Based on estimates of H. Brammer, "The Potential for Rainfed HYV Rice
Cultivation in Bangladesh" Credit No. 409-BD, 1974.



- 24 -
Marketing and Processing
6.04      The project thanas presently have a food grain deficit of about
10-20% of the total demand. The 280,000 ton increase in paddy production
would turn this deficit into a marginal surplus by 1985, taking into account
the projected population increases. With the prospect of continuing food
shortages for Bangladesh, marketing of any disposable surplus would pose
no problem.
6.05      The fairly active private grain dealers are expected to provide
the additional paddy storage and milling required by the project. The
credit schemes for small paddy driers and husking machines under the proj-
ect would further help to meet demand for additional processing facilities
in the villages.
Prices
6.06      Prices for rice, jute and farm inputs used in this report are
derived from the Bank's projected 1985 world market prices expressed in
1975 constant prices. Production of crops other than rice and jute is
relatively small and most of the output is consumed locally.  Projected
prices for these products are based on the historical ratios between their
prices and the rice price. These ratios have been fairly constant during
the last decade.
6.07      Based on a projected world market price of US$325 per ton fob
Bangkok for high grade rice (5% broken), the price for low to medium
quality rice (25%-35% broken) cif Chittagong is estimated at US$255 per
ton. After allowance for transport and handling, the equivalent farm
gate price is Tk 85 per maund (82.5 lb) of aman paddy, which is the
preferred crop. Boro and aus are priced somewhat lower. The estimated
farm gate price for jute (Tk 115 per maund) is based on a projected world
market price of US$405 per ton.
6.08      For economic analysis, the official exchange rate of US$1 = Tk 13
has been adjusted to a shadow exchange rate of US$1 - Tk 16 to reflect the
foreign exchange scarcity. The assumptions behind the shadow exchange rate
and all projected prices used in the financial and economic analysis are
discussed in Annex 14.
Farm Income
6.09      Present and projected farm budgets have been analyzed on the basis
of three representative farm sizes: 1.5 ac, 4.0 ac and 8.0 ac (Annex 15).
Although some off farm earning opportunities exist, only the value of crop
production has been included as income in the analysis. Because GOB intends
to eliminate subsidies for agricultural inputs, the analysis assumes that no
subsidies exist in the future. The znnual net incomes from farming, including
the imputed value of produce consumed on the farm (in 1975 prices), for the
three representative farm types are:

Farm Size        (Tk)             (Tk)                Percent
1.5         2,450               4,300                75
4.0         6,270               9,800                56
8.0        10,200              15,500                52
In the derivation of these farm incomes, cash payments for hired labor and
inputs, including the operating and capital recovery costs of low-lift pump/
shallow tubewells has been charged as production costs.
VII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
General
7.01      Bangladesh presently imports about 20% of its food grain demand
and this severely strains its already precarious balance of payments situa-
tion. A direct project benefit from the increased rice production would
consequently be the foreign exchange savings. The project would also in-
crease the draft animal power supply and production of fish which forms 80%
of protein supply in the Bengali diet. The cultivators in the project area
would benefit from increased farm incomes. The landless laborers and the
underemployed marginal farmers would benefit from increased employment
opportunities, from intensified agricultural and fishery activities, rural
works programs, and secondary services such as transportation, marketing
and processing. The rural population in the area would also benefit from
improvements in social infrastructure such as village markets and roads.
7.02      Perhaps even more important is the role of this project as a
prototype for strengthening and revitalizing the country's rural institu-
tions which are vital to improving the conditions of rural Bengalis. A
special feature of the project is that it explicitly aims at reducing the
domination of the rural institutions by "large" farmers, thus making the
institutions responsive to the needs of the small farmers (para 4.08).
Production
7.03      At full development, the project related increased production of
75,000 tons of rice (115,000 tons of paddy, para 6.02) would represent an
annual gross foreign exchange savings of about US$19.0 million. Allowing
for increased foreign exchange costs of fuel, fertilizer, pesticides and
spares and replacements for pumps and tubewells, the net annual foreign
exchange savings due to the project would be about US$15.0 million.
7.04      The project would also have a beneficial effect on the production
of jute and minor crops such as pulses, vegetables, potatoes and oilseeds,
etc. Rehabilitation of the 500 ac of fish ponds and strengthening of the



- 26
fisheries programs in the seven thanaEs would substantially increase the annual
catch. The provision of vaccination services for the draft cattle would
reduce the mortality rate and increase draft power availability. However,
since the project primarily concentrates on rice production, the benefits
for minor crops, fisheries and livestc,ck, were not quantified.
Social Infra-Structure
7.05      The construction and rehabilitation of roads and village markets
would indirectly help in increasing farm production and directly in easing
transporation and marketing pproblems.
Employment Opportunities
7.06      The shift from local varieties to HYV's and the more intensive
land use, would create, at full development, additional annual farm labor
requirements of about 11.2 million man-days or about 25Z over the current
level (Annex 16). However, over the same period, the population and the
labor supply is also likely to increase in about the same proportion and,
accordingly, there is unlikely to be any significant improvement in the
unemployment and underemployment situation currently prevailing in the
project area. Without the project, however, the situation is likely to
get even worse. This merely highlights the population pressure on the
cultivated land in the country and the difficulties in creating new employ-
ment in Bangladesh agriculture.
7.07      The prospects for non-farm employment are considered to be more
hopeful. It is likely that the increased agricultural production, through
backward and forward linkages, would create substantially increased employ-
ment in activities such as marketing, transportation, processing, handicrafts
and other services. During the project implementation there would also be
employment equivalent to about 12,000 man-years created by the RWP.
Income Distribution
7.08      Farms in the project area are somewhat smaller than in most other
parts of Bangladesh and the cultivation practices in the area are somewhat
less developed. Consequently, it is estimated that the present per capita
income in the project area is only about US$70 1/, compared to the national
average of about US$100. Rough calculations indicate that per capita income
of US$75 is about the minimum income necessary to supply the basic require-
ment for subsistence in Bangladesh conditions. It is estimated that only
families owning 2.5 ac or more, constituting some 25% of all households,
have per capita incomes over the "poverty line". Thus, most of the potential
project beneficiaries are poor in the absolute sense.
1/   This estimate is based on a "normal" world market price of US$325
(1975 dollars) per ton fob Bangkok for 5% broken rice.



- 27 -
7.09      With project, it is estimated that the per capita income would
increase to about US$90 at full development in 1985. Assuming, optimis-
tically, that the national average increases about 1% per annum over that
period, the project would still considerably improve the relative income
of the project area farmers - the average per capita income would increase
from about 70% to about 82Z of the national average.
7.10      Currently, in the project thanas, as in the rest of the country, the
allocation of most agricultural inputs such as pumps, wells, credit, fertil-
izer, insecticides, and fuel are dominated by politically influential "large"
farmers. Small farmers, on the other hand, cannot get production requirements
and often have to buy fertilizers on the black market at inflated prices.
This reduces their net returns as well as their incentive to use such inputs.
By ensuring access to these inputs on an equal basis to all farmers, the pro-
ject would act to arrest the gradually worsening situation of the small farmers
in the project thanas.
7.11      The landless and the small farmers would also be indirectly
benefited from increased food grain availability. Generally, these groups
have to purchase a substantial part of their food grain consumption in the
market and are the worst affected by food grain scarcity. Increased local
supplies would mean a downward pressure on the local rice price and hence
an increase in their "real" incomes.
7.12      The increased employment by about 25% in agriculture, and perhaps
substantially higher in non-farm activites, would help the landless labor-
ers to improve their situation somewhat. Because of the rapidly increasing
population, without the project, they would be hard pressed to retain the
status quo".
Economic Rate of Return
7.13      Based on the following assumptions, the project's economic rate of
return is estimated to be over 40% (Annex 17):
(a)  A five-year project implementation and a thirty
year project life;
(b)  full agricultural development five years after
project completion;
(c)  projected 1985 world market prices in terms of
1975 dollars for crops, fuel, fertilizers and
pesticides;
(d)  allowance for the shortage of foreign exchange by
using a shadow rate of US$1 = Tk 16;



- 28 -
(e) all farm labor valued at about 45% of the peak
market wage (see Annex 16) and construction labor
at the market wage;
(f) only the increase in paddy production due to the
project is included in the benefits; benefits
from increased production oE jute, rabi crops,
fisheries and improved livestock are not included
in the analysis; and
(g) inclusion of all project coits except price con-
tingencies, taxes and dutie;4 and short-term agri-
cultural credit; the capita:L cost of low-lift
pumps which are merely a replacement for the
existing pumps is also excluided from the "economic"
cost.
7.14      The project involves a variety of inputs and outputs whose rela-
tionships are defined, at best, vaguely.  Also, many of them are difficult
to "price" appropriately. Accordingly, the margin of error in the ROR esti-
mate is likely to be quite substantial. Tests made to determine the sensi-
tivity of the ROR estimate to various alternative assumptions about costs
and benefits indicate that returns remain high under a variety of adverse
assumptions, e.g. even if the foreign exchange were to be valued at the
official exchange rate (US$1.00 - Tk 13.00) or all the farm labor were to
be priced at the peak market wage rate (Tk 15/man-day), the rate of return
would remain over 40%. Similarly, even if the net benefits were 25% smaller,
or the investment costs 25% higher, or the build-up of project benefits con-
siderably slower, the rate of return would still exceed 40%. Finally, in
the rather unlikely event that the last three conditions occur simultaneously
i.e. the benefits are 25% lower, the costs 25% higher and the build-up of
benefits slower, the rate of return would be about 30%.
7.15      By conventional standards, the returns estimated for this project
are rather high. However, this should not be surprising for, in contrast
to the conventional capital intensive projects, the primary emphasis in
this project is to achieve increased production by promoting a markedly
better technology (rainfed HYV cultivation) through marginal (capital)
investments in institutional improvement. It should be noted though that
the preparation and implementation of projects such as this imposes heavy
demand on trained manpower -- a resource which currently in many developing
countries is perhaps even more scarce than capital.
Project Replicability
7.16      An important issue in projects such as this is the financial,
administrative and technical feasibility of replicating the project in
other areas.  The model developed in this project would be eminently suitable
for most of the country. Financially, the project represents an investment



- 29 -
of about US$100 1/ per rural family spread over a five year period. Exter-
sion of the project to the estimated 10 million rural families in Bangladesh
would represent a cost of about US$1 billion -- a rather substantial sum,
but well within the means of the country, particularly in view of the ex-
pressed interest of other donors in this area. Administratively, the
project is designed to make the maximum use of the institutions and organi-
zations already in existence in the rural areas. A deliberate effort is
made to strengthen and improve the existing institutions rather than to
bypass them by creating new boards or project authorities. It is hoped
that the improved working procedures evolved in this project would be
transferable, quickly and directly, to other areas in the country. The
on-the-job training programs emphasized in great detail in this project
are likely to produce a fairly large cadre of middle-level field staff, 2/
well versed in the planning and implementation of such projects. Finally,
the project is essentially based on the promotion of HYV cultivation under
rainfed conditions and of better utilization of surface and groundwater
through minor irrigation schemes. Both of these have a large potential
in most parts of Bangladesh making the project experience applicable on
a country-wide basis.
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
8.01      Agreement on the following points has been obtained from GOB:
(a) Rural Works Program (para 4.02), Minor Irrigation Program

implementing these components, GOB would employ foreign
consultants for rural credit, local consultants for the
rural works program, and as and when needed, seek technical
assistance from the experts stationed in Bangladesh under
projects being financed from other external assistance
(para 4.09).
(b) During project implementation, BADC and BKB would not sell
on credit any shallow tubewells in the seven project
thanas (para 4.03).
