BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Physical Environment
Location
1.        The four Bogra thanas (Kotwali, Sherpur, Gabtali and Sariakandi)
are about 90 air miles northwest of Dacca, while the three Mymensingh thanas
(Muktagacha, Trisal and Gaffargaon) are about 55 mi north of that city (Map
11599).
Climate
2.        Although Bangladesh lies 20.5° N (the project area, about 24° N)
of the equator and a larger part lies in the temperate zone, the Himalaya
range shields it from cold northern wind and bottles up the monsoon winds
blowing inward from the tropical.Bay of Bengal. Moreover, 90% of its land
lies below 150 ft above sea level.  Bangladesh, therefore, has a warm sub-
tropical monsoon climate rather than a temperate climate. Essential
climatic data of the two Districts are given in Tables 1 and 2.
3.        The project thanas have three main seasons:  the wet season, June
to October, during which occurs aboult 80% of the total annual rainfall; the
dry season, November to February, with very little rainfall and the lowest
temperatures and humidity, and the premonsoon season, March to May, with
the highest temperatures and evaporation, and occasional thunder showers
which provide about 15% of the annu;l rainfall. Even during the wet season
the rainfall is erratic.
4.        The mean annual rainfall '-L about 68 inches for the Bogra thanas
and about 88 inches for the Mymensingh thanas. The temperatures are gen-
erally warm with a mean monthly range of 70-85°F. The daily minimum tem-
perature, however, may drop to 60°F towards later November during cold
spells. Relative humidity is generally high.
Topography
5.        The four Bogra thanas cover about 375,000 ac and are situated in
one contiguous block to the immediate west of the large Jamuna River (the
upper reach of the Brahmaputra), but- they are shielded from the river by
the Brahmaputra embankment. The land slopes generally toward the southwest,
with elevations varying frog 60 ft Jbove mean sea level (MSL) to about 40 ft.
Two medium-large rivers flowing north-south, intersect the area (see Map
11599). The Bengali in Sherpur thana is seasonal with negligible flows
in the dry season.
-32-



ANNEX 1
Page 2
6.        The area is divided into two distinct topographic units.  In the
area west of the Karatoa River (the western part of Kotwali and Sherpur
thanas), the land rises to an elevated terrace, known as the Barind Tract.
About two-thirds of Barind Tract lands are level and one-third, where it
joins the plains to the east, is broadly dissected. The tract is typical
of a large formation of the northwest region of Bangladesh. The land east
of the Karatoa River is level, in a broad sense, but contains old meanders
and numerous sand ridges and depressions (bils), formed by the Teesta,
Jamuna, Karatoa and Bengali river flood actions. The Brahmaputra east
embankment protects the area from flooding by the river, but the area is
regularly inundated during the monsoon season by rainfall and runoff of
the catchment of the tributaries flowing through the area. Periodically,
overflow of the Teesta River descending from the north contributes to the
inundation. Lands east of the Brahmaputra embankment in Sariakandi thana
comprise an extensive area of low lying sandy land (chars) in the main flood
plain.
7.        The three thanas in Mymensingh comprise about 238,000 ac and are
located southwest of the Old Brahmaputra River. The lands lie mostly on
the Old Bralimaputra flood plain where the main channel was until 1787. The
relief comprises the broad silty ridges and basins of a meandering plain.
The southwestern part of Muktagacha and the southern third of Gaffargaon are
on the remains of the Madhupur Tract, an older, slightly elevated and more
dissected area.  M4uktagacha thana is situated between the Banar and the Old
Brahmaputra rivers to the west of the city of Mymensingh (Map 11599). The
nonperennial Shirkali (called the Mluhuri in its lower reaches) flows through
the area between the two perennial rivers. The land is mostly level. Eleva-
tion varies from 45 ft to 40 ft MSL. Fairly numerous bils (low-lying basins
about 8 ft lower than surrounding ground) provide for some micro-relief.
Trisal and Gaffargaon thanas form a contiguous block located south and east
of Mymensingh City, and lie between the Old Brahmaputra and the Muhuri-Banar
River system. These two thanas are separated from the Muktagacha thana by
the Mymensingh Kotwali thana (Map 11599).  The nonperennial Sutia River
crosses Trisal and joins the Banar at the southwest corner of the thana.
Trisal thana is generally flat and gently slopes to the south between eleva-
tions of 35 ft and 20 ft MSL.  Bils and old meanders provide micro-relief.
The main river channels are cut deep into the surrounding plain. At lower
water levels, the Old l3rahmaputra water surface is about 35 ft below the
adjacent land. The northern part of Gaffargaon is similar to Trisal.
Farther south and on the Mladhupur Tract, the topography of the thana be-
comes increasingly broken and complex and is deeply intersected by creeks
that drain into the Banar River.
Soils
8.        The Teesta, Jamuna and Karatoa flood plain of the Bogra thanas
have sandy to silty ridges, broad level silty to clayey depressions, and
sandy char land along the Jamuna River. The Barind Tract has slowly per-
meable soils overlying little-weathered red Madhupur clay. Because of the
elevation, the tract is generally flood free.
-33-



ANNEX 1
Page 3
9.        The Old Brahmaputra flood plain of the Mymensingh thanas has
broad silty ridges and meandering clayey basins, but there are narrow
strips of sandy to very sandy land in Trisal thana next to the Old
Brahmaputra River. The Madhupur Tract of Gaffargaon and Muktagacha thanas
consists mainly of Madhupur clay which gives rise to both loamy and clay
soils, and a red, mottled, pervious clay substratum. It is generally well
drained on higher ridges but poorly drained on lower terraces.
10.       There are no problem scoils in the project area.  All of the soils,
except the very sandy char land, can grow the cereals and other crops
normally grown in Bangladesh. TLe Barind and Madhupur tracts are also
suitable for growing fruit and other trees. The land use pattern of the
flood plains is primarily determined by the normal state of annual flooding
and the water retention ability cf the soils (Annex 2). The raised home-
stead land is used for growing trees and kharif vegetables.
Natural Drainage
11.       Monsoon rains of great intensity cause ponding over large areas
of the project; the water is removed through the area in natural drains
as the main river levels fall. The project areas are reasonably favorably
situated (elevations 60-40 ft MSL) so the natural drains are effective.
Removal of obstructions in the drains and enlargement would increase the
effectiveness of the natural drai:is. An indication of the extent and depth
of flooding in the thanas is illu3trated by the following:

Frequency and duration of inundation is the main constraint on the extent
and intensity of land use for cultivated agriculture.
Water Resources
12.       Ihe surface water resources available for dry season pump irri-
gation in the project thanas are much less than the number of rivers may
suggest.  In the four Bogra thanas the use of water from the Jamuna River
-34-



ANNEX 1
Page 4
is precluded by the Brahmaputra flood embankment (except for the river side
low land in Sariakandi). An unspecified portion of the average 150 cusec
flow of the Bengali River must be reserved for maintaining dry season water
transport. The Karatoa River is non-perennial. In the three Mymensingh
thanas, the Old Brahmaputra is nearly dry in the winter. The lower reaches
of the Kaoraid and Banar rivers and their tributaries are subject to a small
(three inches to six inches) fresh water tidal range during the dry season.
Annual inundation fills the bils and natural drains and provides stored
water for dry season use, estimated at 56,000 ac ft in the four Bogra thanas
and 15,000 ac ft in the three Mymensingh thanas.
13.       Data relating the surface water supply available for dry season
pumping is not sufficient for accurate estimations. The best practical
guide is the fielding of existing pumps. In the Bogra thanas, recent
experience indicates that the limits for low-lift pump use are being neared.
In the Mymensingh thanas, water of existing bils may offer a limited in-
crease in number of pumps.
14.       Groundwater resources are substantial in all seven thanas.  Some
shallow and deep tubewell experience exists in most of the thanas. Good
water-bearing materials are located at depths varying from 30 to 120 ft.
Water depths vary between 12 ft to 20 ft below the ground surface. Annual
recharge is estimated at 20 inches. Water tables near the rivers are ob-
served to rise and fall with the rivers, indicating satisfactory lateral
permeability. The potential exists for the installation of many thousand
wells in the project area and for the wells to overtake the low-lift pumps
as the primary source of irrigation water supply. There should be no
problem in siting the proposed 2,500 wells based on the general location
of the land relative to rivers and land gradient, guided by the performance
of existing wells, and some trial and error.

BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Land Use and Agricultural Production
Land Types and Present Cropping Pattern
1.        The present cropping pattern in the project thanas, as in most
parts of Bangladesh, is determined mainly by land elevation, soil permea-
bility and the location of the land in relation to rivers and depressions.
These factors together determine the normal status of flooding on the land
during the monsoon season. The relation between land, and the flood and
cropping pattern may be generally described as follows (see Table 1):
(a) Flood-free high land, soil sandy, water unpoundable
--broadcast aus (B. aus) or millet in spring, fallow
or kharif vegetables during monsoon, rabi pulses/
oilseeds or fallow during the winter dry season.
(b) Flood free high land, soil silty or silty clayey, water
poundable by bunding--on flood plains, the pattern is B.
aus or jute in spring, fallow during monsoon, rabi during
winter, and on the Barind Tract in Bogra District, aus
or fallow in spring, transplanted aman (follow the fal-
lowed land) during monsoon, and fallow in winter, or
the land is used for orchards or trees.
(c) Medium land with reliable shallow flooding up to 3 ft--
aus or jute in spring, transplanted aman (T. aman) during
monsoon, rabi or fallow during winter.
(d) Medium low land with deep flooding from 3-6 ft--Mixed
aus and broadcast aman (B. aman) in spring (harvest
aus in July/August and B. aman in November) rabi or
fallow in winter.
(e) Low-lying plains with 6 ft flooding or deeper--Inside
the embankment, deepwater aman or boro, outside of
embankments (char land)--aus in spring, fallow during
monsoon, rabi or fallow in winter dry season, or grass-
land or winter grazing, or idle throughout the year.
Rice Growing Seasons
2.        Aus is a rainfed crop sown in March-April on land of all eleva-
tions, and harvested in July-August before deep flood arrives. Broadcast
aman (B. aman) is sown at the same time as aus on low land either singly
or in a mixture with aus or jute, and harvested in November. Transplanted
-38-



ANNEX 2
Page 2
aman (T. aman) is transplanted in July-August on medium high or medium
land which is shallowly flooded, and harrentle  tn Ne-vember-T)ecembrr.  Boro
is transplanted in January-February on low land where irrigation is avail-
able and harvested from May to mid July.
Natural Constraints
3.        Aus, aman and boro have different constraints.  Aus has the big-
gest risks. Unreliable spring rain causes drought hazard at sowing time
and the early growing period (March/April). Floods in July may damage
the maturing aus grown on medium land and severely damage those grown on
low flood plains outside of embankments or char land. The monsoon poses
problems for sun drying aus paddy.
4.        Cold spells in late November may bring temperature to 60°F
or less, damaging flowering plants of late T. aman, and severely reducing
the yield. Depth of water on land at transplanting time and later in the
season determines whether dwarf HYV T. aman can be grown.
5.        Boro rice has the least natural constraints as long as irrigation
is available. Water depth in some despressions at transplanting time (De-
cember) may preclude the use of HYV. Rain may also interfere in the sun
drying of late boros.
6.        No problem soils exist in the project thanas.  Bangladesh farmers
generally weed rice fields quite clean by manual methods. They have their
normal share of disease and insect problems 1/ and practice minimal control
methods. On-farm paddy storage in tall bamboo/clay bins is satisfactory
provided the paddy is properly dried. However, since aus and late boro
have drying problems, the loss during storage from weevils and beetles
is considerable. Seed storage needs improvement, especially for boro and
aus seeds which must be stored through the monsoon period.
Present Production
7.        Of a total gross land area of 612,500 ac, 76% (467,000 ac) are culti-
vated in the seven thanas. The prospect of further expansion of cultivated
land is limited. The present total cropped area is 745,000 ac, giving a
160% cropping intensity. Further increase in cropping intensity has only
a moderate scope.
1/   The Bangladesh Rice Research Institute (BRRI) tests new selections or
varieties against the following:
Diseases  --  Bacterial leaf and sheath blight, bacterial leaf streak
tungro virus, bakane, foot rot, seedling blight, rice
blast, leaf scald and sheath rot.
Insects   --  Stem borer, leaf roller, gallmidge.                   -39-

Project Approach
13.       The project approach to increasing food production in the project
thanas will include:
(a)  Accelerating the replacement of local rainfed aus
and T. aman by HYV.
(b)  Providing minor irrigation facilities for expanding
boro rice and vegetable areas during the rabi sea-
son.
14.       The Government has already an on-going Accelerated Rice Production
Program (ARPP) based on extension of rainfed aus and T. aman, and UNDP has
provided a senior land use advisor to assist the Ministry of Agriculture in
this endeavor. The land area potentially suitable for planting HYV in the
seven project thanas has been estimated to be 218,000 ac for aus and 221,500
ac for T. aman. Thana breakdowns are given in Table 4.
15.       To be conservative, the project aims at reaching only 50% and
75% of the potential areas of HYV aus and HYV T. aman respectively by the
tenth year of project implementation.
16.       The project will take the following specific steps to ensure that
the target will be reached:
(a)  The project will receive the HYV technology package to
be put through the national extension program under the
IDA Agricultural and Rural Training project and the UNDP/
FAO Extension project, but the technology is expected to
be adopted in the project thanas at a faster pace through
improved coordination between the extension and IRDP/TCCA
officers.
(b) In addition, the following practices will be introduced
for KSS-wide adoption:
-42-



ANNEX 2
Page 6
(i) Selection of land suitable for planting T. aus
and T. aman. Criteria will be developed under
the project with the help of the KSS membership
register and KSS land maps (Annex 7, para 18).
(ii) Good nursery management and proper ways of handling
seedling. The purpose is to obtain uniform and
vigorous seedlings with intact roots to effect
quick revival of seedlings after transplanting
and rapid early growth of the young plants. Rapid
root penetration into greater depth will help aus
to withstand possible drought. Rapid growth of
young plants will help HYV T. aman to beat the
rising flood. Overall, it will enable rice plants
to have a higher percentage of effective tillers,
lower percentage of unripened grains at harvest,
and therefore a higher yield (Table 5).
(iii) As a part of good nursery management, seed dis-
infection and nursery pest control which costs only
a little will be initially introduced to ensure
only healthy seedlings are transplanted into main
fields. Main field pest control will be promoted
after small farmers are accustomed to chemicals
and applicators.
(iv) KSSs will be given initial small lots of high
quality HYV seeds and organize their own multipli-
cation, so that all KSS land suitable for HYV will
be fully planted quickly.
(v) Selection of varieties and planting dates. Initially,
taller but earlier aus HYV varieties will be promoted,
particularly those with vigorous seedling growth.
The T. aman varieties planting dates will be recom-
mended on a basis of completing flowering before
mid-November to avoid damage by cold spells.
(vi) The IRRI-designed one or two ton paddy driers will
be promoted in project thanas with the support of
medium-term rural credit to be provided under the
project.
(vii) The project will ensure that TCCA-KSS members will
receive their supply of fertilizers at official -
prices (they are not buying at these prices at present
due to the faulty distribution system). TCCA-KSS
members are expected to use adequate amounts of
fertilizers earlier than farmers not under the
project's intensified extension program.
-43-



ANNEX 2
Page 7
(viii)  Experts of the UNDP Extension Project and
the IRRI Team at BRRE will provide technical
assistance to the above work items.
(ix)  The TCCA-KSS members will have access to short-
term and medium-term rural credit (Annex 6).
17.       In view of the extension of HYV T. aman and aus varieties since
1970, it is projected that even without the project there would be a sig-
nificant increase in production. However, with the project, HYV area and
average yields (Table 5) are expected to increase at a faster pace (Table 6).
At full development, the annual padcy production is projected to reach 660,000
tons, as compared to the present production of 380,000 tons and a projected
production of 545,000 tons under "without project" conditions. At 65% con-
version rate, the annual rice production at full development would be about
430,000 tons compared to the current: production of 245,000 tons and a "with-
out project" estimated production of 355,000 tons. The project related in-
creased production would be about 75,000 tons of rice annually. Detailed
projections for each thana are giverL in Table 7 of this Annex.

ANNEX 3
Page 1
BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Rural Works Program
Background
1.        The Rural Works Program (RWP) began in Bangladesh in 1962 with
US PL-480 assistance. The program had provided some 170 million man-
days of employment to landless people between 1962-68. Work was concen-
trated on road construction, minor irrigation and drainage earth moving
works. Most of the farm-to-market roads in the project areas were con-
structed under RWP with labor intensive methods during that period.
2.        During that period, rural works projects were proposed through
local initiative at the union level and formulated into thana plans. Each
thana had prepared a thana plan book which contained maps showing works to
be done. These maps still exist in the thana offices. Many proposed works
in the seven project thanas remain to be done.
3.        Since 1970, funding to RWP has been negligible although RWP is
still considered an important item of GOB's rural development program.
Progress in formerly proposed works has been severely impaired due to the
War of Independence in 1971 and periodic flooding. Particularly affected
by lack of maintenance in that time were the roads (mainly dirt roads).
Top priority before new works are begun is that existing roads be
repaired.
4.        Official procedures for RWP (i.e. Ministry of Local Government,
Rural Development and Cooperatives--Memo No. E/1/1S-3/74/602(90), dated
August 6, 1974), were adequate for their purposes. But these announced
procedures have not been followed, resulting in the stagnation of RWP in
recent years. The failures are specifically attributable to the following
reasons:
(a) Selection of work sites is based on local political
pressures rather than actual needs.
(b) Delays in processing union proposals causes late
approval and fund appropriation.
(c) When funds reach the thana/union shortly before
the end of dry season; the works either are
not started at all, or are done hurriedly re-
sulting in poor quality.
-53-



ANNEX 3
Page 2
(d) Escalation of prices occurring between time of planning
and receipt of the funds makes it impossible to complete
all the proposed works. As a result, work is often
not started since time does not permit request for change
of plans and budget. Also, officers in charge frequently
do not want to offend any pressure group by eliminating
some work items included in the original plan.
(e) Reduced results have led to successive budget reductions
during the years 1972 to 1974.
Project Approach
5.        The project approach is to improve the selection of look sites and
then to enforce strictly GOB's own official procedure for RWP.
6.        Detailed timetables for preparing designs and estimates, float-
ing tenders, selecting bids, appropriating funds, recruiting landless
laborers, beginning and completing construction, and inspections were agreed
upon. Officers responsible for doing each phase of the work are specified,
and timetables are provided (paras 17 and 18).
7.        Local consulting firms w:Lll be employed to assist the Ministry of
Local Government, Rural Development and Cooperatives in overseeing project
implementation. The IDA staff res:Ldent in Bangladesh would be available for
consultation with the Ministry and the local consultants.
Project Works
8.        Pucca (paved) Roads - A 15-mile long District council road from
Bogra through Gabtali and Sariakandi to the Brahmaputra embankment will be
upgraded by the project (see map 1600). About 5.5 mi of the road are brick
soled and in poor repair. The remaining 9.5 mi are dirt (katcha). Under
the project, the brick surface in t:he first 5.5 mi (Bogra to Gabtali) will
be rehabilitated and surfaced with bituminous carpet (2-1/2 to 3 inches mat
of broken brick chips and bituminous asphalt).  The 9.5 mi from Gabtali to
the Brahmaputra embankment will be formed to grade and paved with brick
(8 inches herringbone in two layers of brick) surface 12 ft wide with 5 ft
shoulders on each side and a 2:1 slope.  The road surface will be built at
1.5 ft above high flood water level. The 15 minor bridges will each be
equivalent to a 10 ft span for the paved road section, with brick abutments
and a reinforced concrete deck. One or a combination of several units will
be adequate for any crossing except: the Bengali River which is about 600 ft
wide at the Sariakandi crossing where the existing ferries will still have
to be used.
9.        Katcha (Dirt) Roads - The project would improve 300 mi of union
roads, covering about 50% of all existing roads in the seven thanas. The
roads selected for improvement are shown in maps 1600, 11601 and 11615. In
-54-



ANNEX 3
Page 3
general, the criteria for selection of the road to be improved favored roads
connecting the larger and most populated villages and roads that provided
access to the paved District roads and roads providing travel between thanas.
10.       Improvements will provide for a standard 16 ft road surface of
compacted earth. The road will be 1.5 ft above the high water level. The
road rehabilitation program will bring the chosen existing roads to de-
sirable standards. A lack of cross drainage has led farmers to cut the
roads and this contributed to the disrepair of katcha roads. The project
will provide two culverts per mile of road or a total 600 culverts, which
appears sufficient to adequately correct the cross drainage problems.
11.       The distribution of katcha roads in the seven thanas is approxi-
mately as follows:
Thana                Miles           Thanas       Miles
Bogra Kotwali               45            Muktagacha      40
Sherpur                     30            Trisal          45
Gabtali                     55            Gaffargaon      75
Sariakandi                  10            Total          300
12.       Rural Markets - Bi-weekly rural markets congregate in the open
air in or near towns. Frequent rains during the monsoon disrupt the markets
and damage the quality of the produce. This forces farmers to sell at low
prices. Some forty covered rural markets will be built in seven thanas with
five each in Sherpur and Trisal and six each in the other five thanas. The
exact location of the markets will be decided by the Thana Project Executive
Committees in consultation with IDA.
13.       The 40 markets will each have 5,000 sq ft of paved ground and a
corrugated galvanized sheet iron roof. The pavement will consist of a
bricksoling surfaced with 1-1/2 inches of cement mortar screed. Surround-
ing the paved area will be 10,000 sq ft of graded area and 2,000 linear ft
of drains. A 1-1/2 cusec tubewell (with pucca surrounding pad) will be
provided adjacent to the paved shelter areas to furnish drinking water
and washdown water for the vegetable and fish sections of the market.
14.       Drainage Channels - Natural drains provide for surface drainage
in the project area. The existing drains, however, have become obstructed
by man-made small dikes, bank cave-ins, silting, debris, etc, resulting in
ineffective surface water removal. The standing flood is ponded over adja-
cent areas for a longer period. It erodes roads, delays harvest of aman
crops and planting of rabi crops. Improvement of these drains will increase
their water removal capacity during the monsoon season, and water storing
capacity for dry season uses for irrigation.
-55-



ANNEX 3
Page 4
15.       The drain improvement work involves excavation which is highly
labor intensive.  .ie wou& must Je done during the dry months which fits
in with the farm labor slack season. The drains identified by the CP
mission for imprcvement  mder thga project are shown in maps 1600, 11601
and 11615. These natural channels, however, vary in cross-section and the
need of repair from section to section. About 54 mi of drains in five of
the seven thanas will be improved:
Items                     Improved Drain
(miles)

The work will involve an estimated 45 million cu ft of excavation. The
exact work sites and volume of earth moving will be determined by surveys
done by the District and thana engineers before bidding (para 18).
16.       Fish Ponds - The present status and plan for fish production
in the ponds are discussed in Annex 10. The RWP concerns only the physical
rehabilitation work of about 500 ac of fish ponds in the seven thanas, to
make fish culture possible.  Tentatively, 100 ac each will be rehabilitated
in Kotwali, Sherpur and Sariakandi thanas and 50 ac each in the other four
thanas. The work will consist of dewatering, dredging and deepening, bottom
improvement, repairing the embankment, proper sloping and removal of hyacinth
and other water plants. The rehabilitation will be started after the thana
fishery officers have surveyed and selected the ponds, and IRDP has organized
landless people in surrounding villages into TCCA fishery cooperatives to
manage the reclaimed ponds.

Hiring of Local Consulting Firms
19.       In view of the Ministry's small engineering staff, local consult-
ing firms of repute (one for Bogra and one for Mymensingh) would be hired
to assist the Ministry in:
(a) reviewing design and cost estimates for tendering;
(b) reviewing the tender documents;
(c) reviewing the bid selection by the District and thauas;
(d) inspecting field work at appropriate times;
(e) spot-checking quality of the work after its acceptance
by District and thanas; and
(f) submitting a report after the end of the season, on the
work performance and recommending improvements for next
season s program.
The firms would do the work (a) through (e) above in the field according to
time tables given above. Firms would be responsible to the Joint Secretary
of the Ministry in charge of the Rural Works Program, and the IDA staff
resident in Bangladesh would be available for consultation with the Ministry
and the firms, and be kept informed of the work progress at all times. The
cost of hiring the firms would be charged against the 10% engineering and
administrative cost of Rural Works Program and thana facilities.

BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Minor Irrigation
Background
1.        The Thana Irrigation Program (TIP) began in 1967 as part of the
expanded Rural Works Program. Primarily, the program was initiated to in-
crease irrigation through the use of low-lift pumps by cooperative irriga-
tion pump groups. Procedures and regulations governing the functions, or-
ganization and cooperatives of the pump cooperatives are presented in "The
Manual on Thana Irrigation Programme," June 1968. 1/ The pump group coopera-
tives are landowners organized to irrigate 50-70 ac of land using low-lift
pumps obtained on an annual rental basis from the Bangladesh Agricultural
Development Corporation (BADC). Since 1966, some 40,000 low-lift pumps
have been fielded by BADC in the country resulting in the irrigation of
an estimated 1 million ac of dry season rice (boro).
2.        The BADC is in the process of installing several thousand deep
tubewells (2 cusec capacity) in favorable pumping zones. These tubewells
are made available on an annual rental basis to cooperative groups that
irrigate 100 ac. The agency is initiating a similar program of instal-
ling shallow tubewells. Shallow tubewells with 1/2 cusec pumping
capacity can serve small pump groups irrigating from 10-15 ac.
Present Status in Project Thanas
3.        Low-lift pumps - The distribution of pump groups in the seven
thanas, according to IRDP and the Registrar of Cooperatives, are as
follows:
1/   "The Manual on Thana Irrigation Programme," (Basic Democracies & Local
Government Department, the former Government of East Pakistan, June 21,
1968).
-62-



ANNEX 4
Page 2
Thana                TCCA-KSS       Non TCCA-KSS         Total
Pump Groups     Pump Groups
Kotwali                    2              50               52
Sherpur                    8              65               73
Gabtali                   34             105              139
Sariakandi                54              55              109
Sub-total, Bogra          98             275              373
Muktagacha                15              33               48
Trisal                    17              48               65
Gaffargaon                55             226              281
Sub-total                 87             307              394
Total, 7 thanas          185             582              767
However, according to BADC, the number of pumps fielded for the 1974/75
boro crop has increased by 38 to a total of 805 pumps in the seven project
thanas, of which 680 are 2 cusecs and 125, are one-cusec. The 805 pumps
have a total capacity of 1,485 cusecs and irrigate about 29,000 ac, aver-
aging 19.5 ac per cusec.
4.        Low-lift pumps are located along natural drains and around the
bils and depressions that retain water for long periods during the dry
season. The pumps generally lift water approximately 15 ft except in
Muktagacha and Gaffargaon thanas along the Old Brahmaputra River where
pumps located on the river bank lift water 30 ft from the river.
5.        Since the beginning of the BADC low-lift pump rental program, the
number of acres served per cusec has consistently dropped from 38.5 ac in
1962/63 to 19.4 ac in 1971/72 as the number of pumps increased from 2,024
and 24,254 during the same period. This is due to many reasons -- two have
been the unwillingness of pump groups to include other farmers' land and
the pressure they put on thana and District authorities to forego official
criteria for approving the allocation of pumps.
6.        The Manual on TIP provided instructions on criteria for screen-
ing pump group applications; for approving allocation of pumps according
to a set of criteria including availability of and accessibility to
water sources; topography of the command area; pattern of water dis-
tribution, and seepage factors, etc. The current standing procedures
require each group's applicant to provide a map of the land to be irri-
gated, a list of farmers belonging to the group and the land they each
own in the pump command area.
-63-



ANNEX 4
Page 3
7.        Applications are submitted to a Thana Approving Authority.  The
Authority is assisted by a Thana Irrigation Team which is supposed to in-
spect the sites of each application. The Authority rates the applications
according to a set of criteria and then submits, to the District Approving
Authority, a list of applicants with the highest ratings to receive pumps
at 10% more than the number of pumps allocated to the thana. The District
Approving Authority reviews the Thana Authority's report and makes final
allocation to each thana. The composition of the team and authorities, and
BADC guidelines for screening the applications are given in Exhibit 1 of this
Annex.
8.        The official procedures do contain adequate provisions for keeping
up the efficient use of pumps and giving equitable opportunity for large and
small farmers' land to be irrigated, However, by and large the official cri-
teria are being ignored. Also, the official criteria have been amended by a
number of administrative orders which introduced much flexibility and, at
times, contradictions. The requirecL command area of a 2 cusec pump began at
60-70 ac, but has been reduced at various times until the actually irrigated
area presently averages less than 40 ac.
9.        At the root of such irregularities is the pressure put by local
influential people on the thana, District and Central level officers to
bend criteria so that pumps will be allocated to farmer groups which they
support. The latter are dominated by larger farmers who tend to limit the
number of farmers belonging to the group and hence the area irrigated.
Submitting to such pressure by thana and District officers is one of the
causes of the successive reduction of area irrigated per pump or per cusec
as reported by BADC.
10.       At the time of appraisal, almost all the pumps were in thana
workshops. Only in Gaffargaon thana were the pumps actually being issued
to the pump groups for the coming boro season. There are many makes of
pumps and engines from many different countries. All of the engines and
pumps were relatively old, the newest appeared to be three or four years
old. A limited number of new pumps were expected to be available for the
coming boro season. Almost all of thie pumps that were returned to the
thana workshop for repair were in a condition to be returned to the field.
Less than 2% of the pumps were not ready for service because of lack of
spare parts. The BADC provides mechanics at the thana workshop and they
do the first level of maintenance. 'tepairs that cannot be done by the
mechanic in the thana workshop is sent to the zonal workshops of BADC where
spare parts, shop equipment and repa:Lr staff are available for major over-
hauls and reconditioning of the pumpn; and engines.
11.       The BADC maintains diesel :uel and lubrication stores at the
thana level workshops. The pump groups pay for diesel fuel, lubricants,
spare parts, field maintenance and ovrerhauling service for the pumps pro-
vided by BADC.
-64-



ANNEX 4
Page 4
12.       The BADC, each year before the boro season, announces the availa-
bility of low-lift pumps and stipulates the conditions for rental. For the
1974-75 season, a rental of Tk 600 annually for a 2 cusec power pump must
be paid before delivery of the pump. The pump group also pays a Tk 300
security deposit before delivery of the pump. The deposit is refunded at
the end of the season. The price of spares necessary for repair may be
deducted from the security deposit. The group appoints a driver trained
by and satisfactory to BADC. The driver's salary is the responsibility of
the pump group. Although not stated in regulations, in practice the pumps
furnished by BADC this season normally will be "earmarked" for the same
group in following years.
13.       Discussions of pump performance with farmers in the pump groups
indicated several problem areas. Some pump breakdowns have occurred; in
general, repairs have been made before damage to the irrigated crop has
occurred. The delay in repair invariably occurred because sufficient spare
parts were not available at the thana workshop and sometimes not even at the
zonal workshop. The principal uncertainty during the past irrigation sea-
son was the frequent shortage of diesel fuel. The pump groups interviewed
generally hold the view that early in the season, operation was usually
plagued with minor malfunctioning of the pumps and that as the irrigation
season goes on, a continuous supply of diesel fuel becomes increasingly
more difficult.
14.       Shallow tubewells - In February 1975, BADC initiated a scheme to
sell shallow tubewells to farmers or cooperative groups. Under the scheme,
the buyers pay BADC a 10% downpayment and the balance of the cost of the
well in three equal annual installments. The BADC announces standard
cost of the wells, which varies with size of wells (3 inches and 4 inches)
and depth (80 ft - 150 ft). The highest cost is 1.56 times that of the
lowest cost as estimated by BADC In January, 1975. The main problem with
the scbeme is that BADC's involvement in credit operations would place an
additional burden on the already heavy workload of its field staff, dis-
tracting them from their primary responsibility of providing technical
services. Another problem would be that the beneficiaries of the scheme
are likely to be large farmers only.
15.       Hand pumps - The hand pumps for irrigating crops became popular in
the project thanas only in the last few years. The same hand pump installed
for drinking water tubewells with UNICEF assistance is fitted to a 1-1/2 inch
diameter shallow well and pumped manually to provide irrigation water to
about one-third of an acre. The equipment and material include a standard
No. 6 cast iron hand pump, 1-1/2 inch diameter galvanized iron pipes in 20 ft
sections, a six ft long 1-1/2 inch diameter brass well strainer and sand
pack around the strainer. All these are readily available in thana head-
quarters town shops.
16.       The well depth varies from about 25 to 50 ft.  Farmers can install
the well equipment by themselves by sludger method in one hour, and can move
them from one parcel to another. By rotating among parcels, some farmers
-65-



ANNEX 4
Page 5
irrigate rice up to even 1 ac.  A French volunteer team (Brother of All Men
International-lAM) is currently promoting (among other improvemnts) vith
promising results the use of hand-pumps for irrigating vegetables and other
crops in one union of the Sariakanti thana.
Project Works
17.       The proposed project wilL provide for replacement of 805 existing
low-lift pumps (mostly 2 cusec) with 1,400 new 1 cusec pumps, develop 2,500
shallow tubewells, and 5,000 hand pumps.
18.       Low-lift pumps - The existing pumps are mostly 2 cusec powered
with 16-19 h.p. diesel engines. About 10% of the pumps are 1 cusec powered
with 10 hp diesel engines. The BAIC has favored purchase of the 2 cusec
pumps and has not purchased 1 cusec pumps since 1970. For pumping service
from the mainstreams, e.g. Old Brabmaputra in Gaffargaon and Muktagacha
thanas where the lift can reach 30 ft, the large 16-19 hp engines of the
existing fielded pump are reasonable. However, most of the low-lift pumps
in the project thanas lift the water about 15 ft, for which the existing
engines are excessively large. In all cases, only a fraction of the pump
capacity is needed to irrigate the lands tied to the pump.
19.       The CP mission has suggested replacement of most existing pumps
with a 1-1/2 cusec pump powered with a 5.5 hp diesel engine. A pump-engine
set of this general size with a 5-7 hp engine could be assembled within a
total weight limit of 220 lbs. The unit would be highly mobile and could
be used between several small groups where the lands are not contiguous or
cannot be served from one source location. The appraisal mission favored
the suggestion of the CP mission and following discussions with BADC obtained
an agreement to replace all existing low-lift pumps with 1,400 one cusec
pumps powered with 5 hp diesel engines. The total number of cusecs will be
reduced from 1,485 to 1,400 but irrigated area per cusec is expected to in-
crease from 19.5 ac to 26 ac, and the total area from 29,009 ac to 36,000 ac.
20.       The BADC will, as it does for the national program, procure and,
field the pumps, supply fuel and oil, provide, repair and overhaul services
and train pump operators. The collection of pump rental and other charges
under the project will be consistent with BADC's on-going national program.
The BADC will not distribute during the project period new low-lift pumps in
the project thanas other than those supplied by the project, nor would it
redistribute old pumps displaced by the new pumps in the project thanas. It
will assure the supply of adequate amounts of fuel, oil and spare parts to
the pumps in the project thanas during the pump season.
21.       The system of application and approval of allocating the low-lift
pumps under the project will be as follows:
(a) All new 1 cusec pumps supplied by the project and
old 2 cusec pumps before they are replaced by the
new pumps will be distributed only to TCCA-KSS
-66-



ANNEX 4
Page 6
pump groups. The BADC will not distribute any
other pumps in the seven project thanas to non-TCCA
farmers. All existing non-TCCA pump groups will
be reorganized into TCCA-KSS pump groups under the
supervision of Thana Cooperative and IRDP officers.
Details about this reorganization are given in paras
16 and 18 of Annex 6.
(b) The TCCA-KSS pump group will prepare an irrigation plan
consistent with the KSS production plan and complete a
power pumps application consistent with an adopted proforma
which would include the following: a map of the land
parcels to be irrigated by the pump; a name list of farmers
belonging to the group and the land within the command
area owned by each; total area actually irrigated last
year; boro season and area proposed to be irrigated this
season, total HYV area planted last year boro season and
total HYV area proposed to be planted this year, and
record of repayment of the cooperative rural credit of
the previous year by each member of the group.
(c) The TCCA will assemble the proposed power pump applications
and irrigation plans and the IRDP Project Officer (TPO)
will assure that the information furnished by each of the
KSS pump groups is complete and verifiable.
(d) The power pump applications and irrigation plans will be
transmitted by the TCCA to the Thana Irrigation Team. The
Team will consist of the following members: Thana
Irrigation Officer (Convener); IRDP Project Officer; Thana
Agricultural Officer, and Sectional Officer of BADC, and
a representative of the Water Development Board, if avail-
able. The Team will prepare, annually, a thana map showing
available water resources in the thana for the fielding of
power pumps. Upon receiving pump applications from the
TCCA, it will eliminate all applicant groups whose record
of repayment of cooperative rural credit of last year was
less than 75%. It will then order priority of the application
groups by the following system:
(i) assign one point for each acre actually irrigated
by the pump;
(ii) assign one and a half points for each farmer served by
the pump;
(iii) assign one point for each acre planted to HYV;
-67-



ANNEX 4
Page 7
(iv) assign one to ten poLints for 1 cusec pumps and 1
to 15 points for 2 cusec pumps representing poor
to excellent timely payment of rural credit;
(v) assign one to five points for 1 cusec pumps and one
to ten points for 2 cusec pumps representing poor
to excellent upkeep of pumps during the preceding
season; and
(vi) establish priority of each pump group by the total
of points accumulated by the sum of (i) through (v).
(e)  The Thana Irrigation Team would submit the priority list and
other accompanying documents to the Thana Approving Authority
chaired by the Circle Officer (Development), for transmit-
tal to the District Approving Authority. The Thana Approving
Authority would consist of the same membership as the Thana
Irrigation Team, but the Circle Officer (Development) *ill
serve as convener and the Thana Irrigation Officer, BADC, as
the secretary.
(f) The District Approving Aut:hority would consist of:
Deputy Commissioner/Additional
Deputy Commissioner (Develop-
ment)                              -    Chairman
Executive Engineer,                  -    Member Secretary
BADC
Assistant Director,                  -    Member
Rural Development

The District Approving Authority will examine the schemes
submitted to them for final approval. In doing so, the
following factors should be given high priority;
(i) technical feasibility of projects and water resources;
(ii) appropriate co-ordination among the projects submitted
by different thanas to avoid scarcity of water du& to
concentration of too nany low-lift pumps in one area;
and
-68-



ANNEX 4
Page 8
(iii) per cusec coverage of the low-lift pumps during the
last year in different thanas and the proposed ex-
pansion program during the current season. The
efficiency of irrigation during the last season and
availability of future surface water should be the
guiding factors in considering any request for an
additional number of low-lift pumps.
22.       Shallow tubewells - The BADC will procure, move and store the equip-
ment; site and install the wells; supply fuel, oil, spare parts; offer repair
and overhaul services, and train well operators. The BADC and BKB will not
provide credit to farmers in the project area for procurement of shallow
tubewells.
23.       The following procedures will be adopted:
(a) The BADC Thana Irrigation Officer, under the supervision of
the BADC Executive Engineer (Shallow Tubewells), will identify
favorable groundwater pumping areas in each of the seven
thanas and make this information available to the TCCA.
(b) Following review of the irrigation scheme and
the proposed pump site the BADC Executive Engineer
or his authorized representative will advise
TCCA of the estimated cost of the completed
tubewell and the payments required.
(c) The TCCA will promote, encourage and assist
the formation of shallow tubewell pump groups
by KSSs with a command area of at least 15 ac
per tubewell in the favorable groundwater
pumping areas. The KSS pump group will prepare
and submit to TCCA a map showing land parcels to be
irrigated by the well, and an application including
a name list of member farmers whose land parcels
are involved, area of land farmed by each, area of
different crops to be planted, the total land area
to be irrigated, and the repayment record of previous
cooperative rural credit. The following priority
rating system would be adopted:
(i)  assign two points to each crop acre irrigated by
the well;
(ii) assign three points to each farm household served
by the well; and
(iii) assign two points to each acre of priority crops
to be planted: HYV rice; vegetables; forage, or
fodder crops.
-69-



ANNEX 4
Page 9
(d) The IRDP/TPO and the TCCA will assist the pump
groups of the KSS in the preparation of application
for shallow tubewells to be submitted to BADC,
and for medium-term credit to be submitted to
the Sonali Bank.
(e) Upon payment of the requ.ired 10% down payment
by the KSS, the BADC Executive Engineer will
issue a work order to a contractor (previously
selected contractor for the area) to drill the
well, install the screer, casing, pump set and
fitting, and develop and test the well.
(f) Upon completion of the well installation and
acceptance by the KSS ccncerned, TCCA/KSS will
fully reimburse BADC the balance of the cost
of the well with medium-term credit money
provided by the project through the Sonali
Bank. The detailed financing procedures are
described in para 33(e) of Annex 6.
(g)  During the well development and testing period
and the first several days of actual irrigation
operations, BADC will provide, on the job train-
ing to a selected member of the farmer pump group
to operate and provide the required daily main-
tenance and service of the pump and engine.
(h) At the end of the irrigation season, the pump
will be returned to the shallow tubewell work-
shop for annual inspecti.on, repair and over-
haul.
(i)  Following the first operating season and during
the time the shallow tubewell pump sets are in
the workshops for annual. service and repair,
the BADC will carry out a training program for
minor repairs which all project pump operators
will attend.
24.       The cost of project shallow tubewells will be repaid by the pump
groups to the Sonali Bank in five equal annual installments. The cost of
fuel, lubricants, service, repair and spare parts will be paid by the pump
groups to BADC.
25.       Hand pumps--The project will provide 5,000 hand pumps to individual
small farmers owning up to 0.75 ac: and who are members of TCCA-KSS. Farmers
with smallest holdings will be given priority. Farmers will pay a 10% down
payment and the balance of the cosit of the well equipment will be covered by
the medium-term credit provided by the project through the Sonali Bank.
Farmers will repay the credit in tip to five annual installments.           _70_



ANNEX 4
Page 10
26.       The TCCA would buy the pumps for KSS member applicants through
local bidding and dealers would be paid directly by TCCA. Details of the
credit terms and arrangements are given in para 33(c) of Annex 6. Farmers
growing vegetables and forage/fodder crops will be given priority.
27.       Area irrigable by the 5,000 hand pumps would vary from a minimum of
about 1,700 ac (one third of an ac per pump) to 5,000 ac (one ac per pump).
The benefited area is small, but the project would demonstrate feasibility
of providing medium-term credit to small farmers for purchasing production
equipment for increasing production of high value crops.

BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
(The Following is the Original Text of a
GOB Circular)
Govt. of the People's Republic of Bangladesh
Ministry of Agriculture
CIRCULAR
The Government has decided to field 40,000 low-lift pumps during
1974-75. The following procedures are laid down for the initiation and
approval of schemes:
1.        Thana Irrigation Team
Thana Irrigation Team will be composed of the following:-
1.   Thana Irrigation officer                  -    Convenor
2.   Sectional officer, Water Dev. Board       -    Member
3.   Thana Agriculture officer                 -    Member
4.  T.C.O.                                     -    Member
Functions
a.   It will act as Technical sub-committee of the Thana Approving
Authority in which capacity it will carry out feasibility study
for irrigation schemes based on availability of surface and
ground water, scrutinize the schemes submitted by the Union
Krishi Uhnayan Committee (Union Agricultural Development Com-
mittee - UKUCs) and advise the TAA for according approval to
the power pump schemes. Thana Irrigation Team will be responsible
for collection of all schemes from UKUC.
b.   The Team will prepare a Thana Map showing availability of water
resources in the Thana for fielding of power pump which should
be updated each year.
2.        Thana Approving Authority
The Thana Approving Authority will be composed of the following:
-73-



ANNEX 4
Exhibit 1
Page 2
1.   Circle officer (Dev)                      -    Convenor
2.   Thana Agriculture officer                 -    Member
3.   Project officer, IRDP                     -    Member (In IRDP thanas)
4.   Thana Animal Husbandry officer
5.   Thana Irrigation officer, BADC
6.   Thana Co-operative officer
Functions:
To scrutinise the schemes received from the Thana Irrigation
Team, the following guidelines should be followed:
a.   Map of the Project including the names of the farmers.
b.   The commanded area should be minimum 50 acres for a a 2 cusec low-
lift pump in case of old schemes and 40 acres in case of new
schemes.
C.   At least 60% of the Co-operative loans of the provisions years
should be repaid.
d.   Pump should be voluntarily returned in time for repair to the
nearest BADC store by the group.
Revised terms and conditions on the rental of low-lift pump will
be communicated soon.
After due scrutiny, Thara Approving Authority will approve power
pump schemes in order of priority, with additional 10% over national
allocation made by the District Approving Authority. Their approval of
individual schemes will be subject to final approval by the District
Authority.
District Approving Authority
In each District, there will be a District Approving Authority
consisting of the following:-
1.   Deputy Commissioner/A.D.C.                           Chairman
2.   Dist. Agriculture officer                            Member
3.   Asst. Director, Rural Dev.                            o
4.   Executive Engineer/Asst. Engineer/BADC               Member-Secretary
5.   Assistant Registrar-Co-operative Societies          Member
6.   Assistant Director, Rural Dev.                      Member
7.   District Project Director, IRDP                      Member
All M.Ps should be invited in the meeting of the District
Approving Authority Committee meeting, and their views should be duly
reflected in the final programming.
-74-



ANNEX 4
Exhibit 1
Page 3
Functions:
On receipt of District wise allotment of power pumps, the Dist.
Approving Authority will make thana wise sub-allocation and communicate
the same to the C.O.(Dev), Thana Agriculture officer and Thana Irrigation
officer simultaneously for proceeding with selection of site and collection
of applications for irrigation on power pumps.
The District Approving Authority will examine the schemes sub-
mitted to them for final approval. In doing so, the following factors
should be given high priority:-
a.   Technical feasibility of projects and water resources.
b.   Appropriate co-ordination among the projects submitted by dif-
ferent Thanas should be properly ensured to avoid scarcity of
water due to concentration of too many low-lift pumps in one
area.
c.   Per cusec coverage of the low-lift pumps during the last
year in different Thanas and proposed expansion programme
during the current season. The efficiency of irrigation during
last season and availability of future surface water should be
the guiding factors in considering any request for additional
number of low-lift pumps.
d.   Special attention should be paid to the need for bringing add-
itional areas under cultivation in pump groups which had low
coverage i.e. below 20 acres per cusec during the last season.
e.   Low-lift pump which put in specially bad performance from
efficiency point of view should be excluded from this year's
list. It is suggested that any 2 cusec pump which has covered
25 acres or lesser areas should be omitted from next year's
programme.
f.   Pumps group which did not return low-lift pumps voluntarily for
repairing in time and also defaulted in the repayment of co-
operative loan should be excluded from the list of this year's
programme.
g.   District Approving Authority will make final allocation of pumps
among the Thana after considering the schemes in accordance
with the criteria mentioned above.
The District-wise allocation of low-lift pumps has been made
taking the availability of surface water into consideration and last three
year's performance of the low-lift pumps. The Government reserve the right
of increasing or decreasing allocation to some districts if either pro-
arammes are not finalised within due dates on adequate number of suitable
schemes are not available. The district wise allocation of low-lift
pumps is enclosed together with a time schedule for programme.
Sd/- A.M. Anisuzzaman
Secretary,             -75-

BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Thana Facilities
Background
1.        Thana facilities include office buildings and living quarters
for thana level officers, godowns, workshops and veterinarian dispen-
saries, belonging to various Govermnent agencies or TCCAs. This Annex
deals with these facilities, except the equipment for the veterinarian
dispensaries, which are discussed iia Annex 9.
2.        Modeled after the Comilla approach, Bangladesh has built Thana
Training and Development Centers (TCDC) in a large number of thanas. The
TTDC has a double story buildiug for offices of the Circle Officer (Development),
all line department officers assigned to the thana, the thana overseers/
supervisors (engineering staff for RWP) as well as a meeting and training
hall. Placing officers of all disciplines in one headquarters building and
using them as trainers of TCCA-KSS todel farmers is an innovative feature
of Bangladesh administration. Similar centers are being built in Sri Lanka
(Agricultural Productivity Centers) and Pakistan (Markhaz).
3.        Near by the TTDC, twinplex two-story buildings have been built
and provide living quarters for the line department officers. The office
and dormitory buildings of TTDC in the seven thanas were in various stages
of construction in 1970 when construction was interrupted by civil dis-
turbances. Many buildings have been in use but have been left partly finished
(para 7). The lack of family living quarters has been a hardship to recently
appointed line department officers. Most of the supporting staff such as
Village Extension Officers, vaccinators, and the TCCA inspectors and village
accountants are recruited locally; they usually live with their families.
4.        The Union Agricultural Assistant (UAA) is the front line exten-
sion officer. He is the only civil servant at the union level, but is not
provided with an office or living quarters. Some of them get free room
and board in larger farmers' homes. This subjects them to undue influence.
5.        The BADC owns a fertilizer godown of 400 ton capacity each in
Sherpur, Sariakandi and Muktagacha thanas, but not in the other four
project thanas. The TCCAs have no storage facilities. This has restric-
ted TCCAs from providing marketing services to its members.
6.        The BADC has a workshop and fuel and oil storages in each of
the seven project thanas for serviciag low-lift pumps and tubewells. The
workshops are generally short of working tools.                              -78-



ANNEX 5
Page 2
Project Works
7.        TTDC Buildings -- The project will bring the construction of the
following buildings to completion:
Thana                                Item
Bogra Kotwali             TTDC                       30% incomplete

Twin quarters              50%
8.        TCCA storages -- The project will construct four fertilizer
godowns, for TCCA, one each at Bogra Kotwali, Gabtali, Trisal and Gaffargaon
thanas, and seven general godowns one for each of the seven thanas. The
fertilizer godown can be used for storing BADC fertilizers for the thana.
The TCCA will serve as store-keeper for BADC and manage all the fertilizer
stores in the seven thanas. It will directly distribute fertilizers to
KSS members, but serve as BADC's agent for fertilizers intended for farmers
who are not KSS members. The BADC will pay TCCA a commission according
to standard rates on a per maund basis. The general godown will be used by
TCCA as transit storage and distribution depot for various items such as
seeds, pesticides, hand pumps, spares and farm produce.
9.        The fertilizer godown will have a 400 ton capacity, and a 3,200
sq ft floor space. The general godown will have a 200 ton capacity
and 1,600 sq ft floor space. They both include storage areas and separate
small living quarters for a store keeper and darwan (watchman). The
The buildings will all have 11 inch brick walls and a plastered and re-
inforced concrete floor and roof. The units would be standard and familiar
-79-



ANNEX 5
Page 3
to local construction contractors. Such structures are chosen for their
durability, low maintenance, protection against pilferage and fire hazards,
and preservation quality for holdirLg stored items.
10.       BADC Thana Workshops -- In the seven thanas, the project would
provide these workshops with additional tools and facilities.
Implementation Procedures
11.       The Circle Officer (Development), assisted by Thana Overseers/
Supervisors, will use the existing standard design and specifications for
TTDC buildings. After surveying the unfinished parts of each building, he
will prepare construction plans for bringing them to completion. For TCCA
godowns, he will use the standard design and specification of the existing
BADC godowns with adjustment to dimensions. He will prepare bids and float
tenders for the construction of the Thana buildings. For preparing bids
and float tenders for TCCA godowns, he will consult IRDP/TPO.
12.       The Thana Project Executile Committee will select the successful
bid.  The Circle Officer (Developmenit) will award the contracts.
13.       The Circle Officer (Development) and Thana Overseers/Supervisors
will supervise the construction.
14.       The Circle Officer (Development) will make payments to contractors
and inspect the completed construction for official acceptance. The detailed
steps and time table would be the same as those for RWP as described in
Annex 3, para 18.
15.       The local consulting firms to be hired for the Rural Works Program
component would also assist in executing this component (Annex 3, para 19).

BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Rural Credit
A. Background
Introduction
1.        The project will supply rural credit only to TCCA-KSS members.
The project credit, both short- and medium-term, will be supplied through
the Sonali Bank. The procedures for loan application, approval, bookkeeping
and supervision will be tightened, with a primary objective to break the
habitual willful defaulting by the farmer borrowers.
Selection of Sonali Bank as the Project Rural Credit Lending Institution
2.        The status of the rural credit system and institutions in
Bangladesh were studied by the Bank Agricultural Credit Review Mission
in 1973. A follow-up mission in August/September, 1974 discussed with
the Government the review mission report (No. 461-BD, July 1, 1974). The
report pointed out the basic organizational and financial weaknesses of
the main rural credit institutions: the Bangladesh Cooperative Bank
(Bangladesh Jatiaya Samabaya Bank or BJSB) for short-term credit, and
Bangladesh Krishi Bank (BKB) for medium- and long-term credits, and recom-
mended improvement measures to be taken. It saw the over-riding issue as
being the need to resolve delinquent debts of the Government and coopera-
tive credit institutions, which, in amount and number, have become almost
impossible to manage. On June 30, 1973 loans in arrears as a percentage
of the amount of the loan portfolio were 47X for BKB and 71% for BJSB.
3.        The follow-up mission concluded that until the Government takes
decisive action toward rectifying the credit institutions and the tradi-
tional cooperative system, there seems to be little that the Bank/IDA can
do to assist BKB and BJSB. The mission, however, recommended the follow-
ing interim measures:
(a) Improve the field operation of the rural
credit system, utilizing commercial banks
as channels, to be tried out under the
IDA Rural Development project.
(b) Continue the dialogue with the Govern-
ment and to leave open the offer to
consider helping to finance the tech-
nical assistance they may desire.
-83-



ANNEX 6
Page 2
4.        The above views of the credit review mission report guide the
approach by .ii-h  .          component of this project is designed, and
is the basic reason why the project chooses the Sonali Bank, the largest
commercial bank (now nationalized) for channeling both the medium- and
short-term credit to be provided under the project. In view of BJSB's
problems, the Government, since 1973, has asked the Sonali Bank to pro-
v±de sho-t-term credit to TCCA-KSS members, thus replacing BJSB in this
function.
5.        Besides the problem of krrears, BKB lends medium- and long-
term loans primarily to individual farmers. For many years, over 60%
of BKB's meditum-term credit was fyr purchase of work animals, which
was difficult to verify. During tppraisal, the Government agreed to
have the Sonali Bank handle both :he short- and medium-term loans
provided under the project.
6.        The Sonali Bank current .y has ten regional offices each cover-
ing 1 to 3 Districts and controll:.ng 20 to 45 local branches. The re-
gional offices and branches are at equately staffed with personnel
trained and experienced in the cotmercial banking business. In the
seven project thanas, the Somiali las five branches, one each in
Kotwali, Sherpur, Sariakandi and (affargaon. Muktagacha thana is
being served by a branch office ir the neighboring Mymensingh thana
and Trisal, by the Gaffargaon brarch. In either case, the distance
is within 12 tailes. Fach br8nch cffice is staffed by a minimum of
five:  one manager, _e ;'ashier, cne accountant, two clerks assisting
the cashier and accountants.
7.        Recently? university graduates in agriculture or economics
were assigned to regional offices as inspectors, with the special duty
to deal with agricultural credit c3eration. They have been trained in
the Dacca head office on banking, Presently, one inspector supervises
not more than ten TCCAs. The Bank plans to recruit more inspectors
when necessary.
8.        Since the Sonali began i:s rural credit operations in 1973 with
IRDP/TCCA, the first loan for the 1973 T. aman crop has been 100% recovered
in the seven project thanas. The -ecovery of 1973 T. boro ranged from 100%
in Gabtali, about 95% in Sherpur aid Gaffargaon, 70% in Sariakandi and
Muktagacha, and 537 in Trisal.  Tho recovery of 1974 T. aman, harvested in
December, 1974 was in progress at :he time of appraisal. The above record
was not ideal, but was far better :han the country as a whole. The Sonali
Bank has conducted a survey of problems involved and is making plans to
improve the performance in thanas of low recovery. The use of the Sonali
Bank in the project, however, is n t meant as a precedent which would lead
to Sonali Bank replacing BJSB and IKB as the major rural credit institutions.
Instead, it is meant to use a bank without the burden of long years of over-
due credit to demonstrate a rural (redit field level operation system which
-84-



ANNEX 6
Page 3
integrates closely with other rural development activities. If the system
proves to be successful, and when basic improvements of BJSB and BKB are
carried out, there should be no reason why the same system could not be
carried out through them.
Limiting Recipients of Project Rural Credit to TCCA-KSS Members
9.        The traditional cooperatives are members of the Central Coopera-
tive Banks (CCB) 1/, which, in turn, are members of BJSB. Generally, these
cooperatives are characterized by domination of large farmers and unsatis-
factory management and supervision. To provide loans to unorganized small
farmers directly by the banks would be difficult to supervise even at a
very high administrative cost. It tends to lead to loans going mainly
to large farmers. To channel project rural credit through the Sonali
Bank and the TCCA/KSS cooperative system is in line with the recommenda-
tion of the credit review follov-up mission (para 3(c)) and believed to
be the only way that, with improved procedures and supervision, a healthy
new tradition of rural credit operation at the field level could be estab-
lished for channeling credit to small farmers. If successfully demon-
strated, the system may hopefully be extended to other parts of the
country.
10.       The TCCA-KSS is supervised by the Integrated Rural Development
Program (IRDP) which is a department under the Ministry of Local Government,
Rural Development and Cooperatives. The IRDP is better staffed, quantita-
tively and qualitatively, at the Central, District and thana levels than
the Cooperatives Department which supervises the traditional cooperative
system (Annex 7).
11.       The project will direct the supply of all inputs (low-lift
pumps, shallow tubewells, hand pumps, processing equipment, and re-
claimed public fish ponds) as well as rural credit only to TCCA-KSS
members, and will also greatly strengthen extension (crop and fishery)
and veterinarian services to TCCA-KSS members. Joining TCCA-KSSs will
become very attractive to medium and small farmers.
12.       Therefore, although there were only 626 KSSs in the seven thanas
at the end of 1974, with a total membership of 18,414, representing only
47% of the villages and about 13% of the total "prospective membership"
(para 26, Annex 7 gives a definition) of about 150,000 farm families, it
is projected that 74% of the villages and the prospective membership will
be covered by the fifth year and 100% by the tenth year (Annex 7, Exhibit
3). The project rural credit will reach an increasing proportion of farm-
ers in the project thanas.
1/   There are 62 CCBs in Bangladesh, one for each sub-division.
-85-



ANNEX 6
Page 4
13.       The strategy of the project is therefore on one hand to make
joining TCCA-KSS very attractive to small farmers and on the other hand
to tighten up discipline in distribution of inputs and credit among the
TCCA-KSS members. The IRDP/TCCA/Sonali Bank team-up appears to be the
only channel where such approach could be successfully applied under the
present circumstances.
B. Project :Emplementation
Introduction
14.       The implementation of the rural credit component of the project
will be discussed in the following sequence:
(a)  Settling of outstanding debts of cooperative
members.
(b)  Short- and medium-term credit purposes and
requirements.
(c) Terms and conditions of credit.
(d) Lending procedures.
(e) Guarantee provisions.
(f)  Provisions for natural calamities.
(g)  Improvement of loan records and
documents.
Settling of Outstanding Debts
15.       The project will require, according to guidelines to be prepared
by the Government in agreement with IDA, either full repayment or signing
agreement to reschedule the payment: of outstanding loans as conditions for
eligibility to TCCA-KSS membership and, for existing TCCA-KSS members, for
eligibility to further credit. An inventory of overdues would be made at
the level of primary societies (Union Multi-Purpose Cooperative Society (UMPCS),
TIP-KSS and TCCA-KSS). A recovery campaign would be launched in each of the
seven thanas by the CCB and TCCA staff.
16.       The GOB has already issued a Decree to the effect that all out-
standing overdue Government loans, including cooperative credit must be
repaid, and the concerned institutlons were to report on outstanding overdues.
The debt inventory work in the project thanas would therefore be carried out
-86-



ANNEX 6
Page 5
for members of all cooperatives (UMPCS, TIP-KSS and TCCA-KSS) in the first
year of project implementation. Also in the first year the TIP-KSSs in the
project thanas would be reorganized into TCCA-KSS pump or shallow tubevell
groups according to procedures to be announced by the Ministry. Their
members will have to repay their outstanding debts or sign agreement with
their debtors (CCB or Sonali Bank) for rescheduling the debt repayments,
before they can be admitted to the TCCA-KSS membership. Those who fail to
comply would forfeit their rights to belonging to any pump/well groups in
these thanas.
17.       The same procedures would be applied to members of UMPCS which
are also to be reorganized into TCCA-KSS. Farmers presently not belonging
to any cooperatives and have no outstanding cooperative debts would be
recruited to join TCCA-KSSs. The processing of these farmers would con-
tinue wherever there are applicants.
18.       Rescheduling overdue balances would be done by CCB Thana Coopera-
tive Officers and IRDP/TPO for the borrowers from the traditional primary
societies, and by Sonali Bank and IRDP/TPO for TCCA-KSS members. Re-
scheduling will be done according to the following simplified terms:
Cultivated     Rescheduling
Overdue balance                land area       terms
Less than Tk 300          Less than 1 ac            4 years
1 - 3 ac            3 years
More than 3 ac            2 year
Tk 300-500                Less than 2 ac            4 years
2 - 4 ac            3 years
More than 4 ac            2 years
Over Tk 500               Less than 3 ac             4 years
3 - 5 ac            3 years
More than 5 ac            2 years
19.       Rescheduling would apply to overdues in both principal and interest.
Failure to repay the yearly installments when due, would cause the cancellation
of the rescheduling agreement and privilege to further loans. Legal action
will be taken against defaulters according to Government orders.
-87-



ANNEX 6
Page 6
20.       Agreement has been obtained that as a condition for credit
effectiveness GOB would finalize the guidelines for repayment of outstanding
Institutional debts by farmers, in agreement with IDA.
Short-Term Credit Purposes and Requirements
21.       The short-term credit wiLl cover the cost of cash inputs (mainly
seeds, fertilizer, pesticides and hiring of draft animals) for aus (local
and HYV), T. aman (local and HYV) and boro (local and HYV). For pump/well
groups, it may include fuel, oil and maintenance costs. Cash input require-
ments for other high value crops, i.e. vegetables/jute, sugarcane and fish
culture will also be covered. But B. aman will not be provided with credit
because farmers are not using input:s on it due to high risk and low yields
involved. Nor will labor cost for any crop be covered since small farmers
primarily use family labor.
22.       The credits will be made for a period of six months, except for
sugarcane subject to a maximum extension of two months, when necessary, to
allow waiting for better crop price!s. The per acre credit amounts for
various crops shall be updated, as necessary, to take into account any
changes in the crop inputs prices. However, the credit amount would not
exceed 30% of the gross production value as estimated from the average
crop yield in the thana during the preceding year (as reported by TAO) and
the prevailing market price for the crop.
23.       Currently, the transplanted aman paddy (local and HYV) is by
far the most important crop in the project thanas - accounting for 47%
of the total cropped area compared to about 3% for HYV aus and about
5% for boro (local and HYV). Even though at full development there
would be substantial increase in the acreage under HYV aus and boro,
the basic pattern would still prevail. (The respective percentages
at full development would be 43%, 13% and 12%.) In view of this,
the highest credit demand in any year would be during the aman sea-
son. Accordingly, it is assumed that the short-term credit require-
ments in any year are the same as the credit requirement for the aman
crop in that year i.e. the credit needs for boro, aus and other crops
can be adequately satisfied by recirculating the collections from the
aman crop. Some further assumptions used in estimating the credit
requirements are as follows:
(a)  The change in the acreage of aman crop from present
to that at full development would follow a straight
line pattern;
(b)  area of the aman crop grown  by TCCA-KSS members would
be in proportion to their percentage in the land owning
agricultural households in the project thana;
-88-



ANNEX 6
Page 7
(c) only 70% of the KSS members would borrow during any crop
season, and
(d) 100% recovery of the loans due. Any short falls to be
covered from the Guarantee Fund in the Bangladesh Bank
or from Sonali Bank's own resources.
24.       With the above assumptions, the short-term credit requirements are
projected to increase from a current estimated level of about Tk 3.60 mil-
lion to about Tk 46.20 million in five years. The IDA and Sonali Bank
would each contribute 50% of the incremental lending over the current level
of Tk 3.60 million. The year-wise financing plan for the short-term credit
would approximately be as follows:
