Medium-Term Credit Purposes and Requirements
25.       Primarily, the medium-term credit would be provided for financing
the procurement of shallow tubewells and hand pumps by the TCCA-KSSs or
their members. The costs of these two items included under the minor irri-
gation component of this project are estimated at about Tk 100 million (in-
cluding price contingencies of about Tk 37 million). The project would also
provide fishing nets, gears and for Tk 7 million to TCCA-KSSs for starting
small industries for processing farm produce (e.g. rice husking and rice
drying). At the time of their establishment, most TCCAs in the project
thanas received about Tk 200,000 each from the IRDP for starting small
processing ventures. However, due to the unfamiliarity of the IRDPs field
staff in planning, appraising and implementing viable small projects of
this nature most of those funds are lying unused with the TCCAs. As part
of the project, staff from BRRI and IRDP headquarters, with assistance from
an IRRI farm machinery expert, would provide technical assistance and train-
ing to the IRDP and Sonali Bank field staff in these areas. In case the
-89-



ANNEX 6
Page 8
TCCAs/KSSs in the project thanas are unable to find viable small projects,
the funds allocated for these purposes would be used to finance additional
shallow tubewells and hand pumps. The purchase of draft animals and carts
currently financed by BKB would not be included in the medium-credit
provided under this project. The shallow tubewells would be procured,
sited, installed and serviced by BADC. The other items would be procured
by TCCA-KSSs directly from local markets.
26.       The Sonali Bank would administer the medium-term credit for the
project. The borrowers would pay' 10% of the total cost as down payment,
and Sonali Bank would provide the rest as medium-term credit. To finance
the credit requirement, IDA would disburse against the procurement of
equipment and material by BADC, estimated to equal about 54% of the total
cost; the borrowers' installment repayments during the five year project
implementation period would total about 28% of the total cost; and GOB's
contribution to the revolving funid during the same period would total
about 8% of the total cost. The projected yearly medium-term credit
requirements for the seven thanasu, and the financing plan, are shown in
Table 2. The maturity of the loans would depend upon the repayment capa-
bilities of the borrowers, but would not exceed five years. The projected
yearly medium-term credit requirements for the seven thanas, and the fi-
nancing plan, are shown in Table 2.
Terms and Conditions of Credit
27.       Loan eligibility.  The project would require the applicants to:
--   settle their outstanding debts according to
conditions stated in paras 15 to 19,
-- nhave a farm plan indicating loan repayment
ability, and
--   subscribe to cooperative shares, and thrift
savings as required by TCCA regulations.
28.       Loan Security.  Although the most reliable security stems from a
careful evaluation of the borrower's honesty, farming expertise and annual
credit repayment capacity, short-term loans would be secured by a mortgage
covering the standing crop for which the loan is made. For medium-term loans,
the Sonali Bank will take a lien on all machines or equipment for which the
loan is made.
29.       Loan Repayment.  Short-term loans would mature two months
after harvest time of crop. The credits will be made for a period
of six months for all crops except sugarcane (which would be for 14 months),
subject to a maximum extension of two months, when deemed necessary by
IRDP/Sonali Bank, to allow waitin,g for better crop prices. Medium-term
credit would be repaid, within five years with two payments in each, two-
thirds to be repaid before Septem'ber 31 and one-third before March 31.

31.       Loan application under the project would be prepared with new forms
containing particulars on the borrowers' assets and liabilities. Such infor-

on: land registry; share capitaL; thrift savings, and outstanding debts.
The supporting farm plan would include information on expected production
(Annex 7).

(a)  a loan agreement stipu:Lating the terms and conditions of the
loan:
(i) the date of issue:
(ii) the amount lent;
(iii)  the due date of the schedule of repayment in principal
and interest;
(iv) the crop proceeds from which repayment would have to
be made;
(v)  the use of funds borrowed and the securities guaran-
teeing the loan repayment in principal;
(vi) interest and accessories.
This loan agreement would be made in triplicate. The
original would be kept by KSS, one copy would be handed
over to the KSS's borrowing member, and the second copy
to TCCA at the time KSS applies for a credit.
(b)  a promissory note signed by a KSS borrowing member by
which he binds himself to repay the loan amount at the

Sonali Bank would have 1:o rediscount credits to the
Bangladesh Bank, and
(c) a mortgage deed coverinlg the standing crops on the
land cultivated by the borrower.
Lending Procedures
33.       Loan Disbursements by Sonali Bank.  Loans would be disbursed in
kind through TCCA delivery orders handed over to suppliers except for the
hiring of draft animals in which case the KSSs will be responsible for
screening loan applications. The suppliers may be private input and
machinery dealers, BADC, TCCA or M[inistry of Agriculture.
-92-



ANNEX 6
Page 11
(a) For fertilizers and pesticides to be supplied by BADC
and the Ministry of Agriculture stores, the order would
state terms of delivery and the total quantity to be
delivered to the KSS. The total would be based on
the KSS seasonal production plan and the sanctioned
input quantities for each individual KSS member.
Upon delivery of the inputs by the supplier (or the
KSS goes to godown and takes bulk delivery) the KSS
manager or his authorized representative would sign
the delivery order in triplicates, one copy would be
kept by the KSS, another copy would go to the Sonali
Bank and the original would be kept by the supplier.
The KSS would distribute the inputs to members
according to the amount sanctioned, and each recipient
would sign on his slot on a KSS receipt form. Copies
of the form would be filed with TCCA and the Sonali
Bank and the original would be kept by the KSS.
(b) For pump/tubewell fuel and oil - If such supplies
are to be covered by the short-term credit, the
authorized representative of the TCCA would sign a
supply contract with BADC for its KSS for the entire
pumping season. The BADC would be obliged by the
contact to supply when needed, and would be paid by
TCCA against delivery orders signed by the authorized
representative of the KSS pump or well groups. Rural
credit will not finance stocking of fuel/oil by KSSs.
(c) For hand pumps - IRDP/TCCA would call local tender for
supplying the pumps to KSS members. Each KSS would send
representatives to a dealer, inspect the pump
equipment, take bulk delivery of all pumps for the KSS,
and sign the TCCA delivery order. A copy of the signed
order would be presented by the dealer to the TCCA
for payment. Upon receiving the pump equipment, each
borrowing member would sign on his slot on a KSS
receipt form. Copies of this form would be filed
with TCCA and the Sonali Bank.
(d) For small paddy husking machine and driers - IRDP/TCCA would
call local tender for supplying and installation of such
machines at the borrowing KSS. The TCCA delivery orders
would specify all particulars including terms of delivery
and/or completion of works. Dealers/contractors would be
paid directly by TCCA upon submission of related invoices
and signed acceptance of the goods and/or works by KSS.
(e) For shallow tubewells - In order to give a complete picture,
the disbursement procedures are explained in the following
three steps:
-93-

Page 12
(i) Costs of wells - BADC would provide TCCA an estimated
cost (when procurem,ent costs of well equipment are
known) for the latter to assist KSS to organize well
groups and process medium-term credit applications
to the Sonali Bank. After the well is installed,
tested and accepted by KSS, it would send to TCCA a

(A) foreign expenditures of directly imported

(D) Taxes and duties;
(E) Drilling and installation;

(ii) Payment of the Cost to BADC
(H) Reimbursement on a 100% basis of improved

locally manufactured item (B above) -- IDA
would reimburse against proper documents as
soon as the procurement was effected by BADC.
Reimbursement on a 70% basis for other local

(G above) before BADC starts drilling.

-94-



ANNEX 6
Page 13
34.       Reimbursements to Sonali Bank.  A revolving rural credit account
will be opened in the Sonali Bank in the name of the Rural Development
Project (Rural Credit Fund).   The account vill be subdivided into two sub-
accounts for short- and medium-term credits, respectively. The GOB will
provide the initial funding of Tk 2 million each to the two sub-accounts.
From these sub-accounts, the Sonali Bank will draw funds required by
seasonal loans and medium-term loans made to KSS's borrowing members through
TCCA. All repayments by the borrowers to the Sonali Bank would be passed
on to the respective revolving funds for subsequent use. Additional reple-
nishments to the short-term sub-account would come from IDA reimbursements
against seasonal lending, Government and Sonali Bank contributions and to
the medium-term sub-account from GOB contribution as well as from Sonali
Bank collectionss. Funds accumulated in these sub-accounts at the end of
the project implementation would be used as revolving resources for further
lending operations in the project thanas.
Guarantee Fund
35.       Pursuant to Articles 60 and 61 of the Bangladesh Bank Amendment Act
1974, a Rural Credit Fund and an Agricultural Credit Stabilization Fund have
been established by GOB. The principle of a system of guarantee has been
laid down. The implementation of the guarantee was started by the Govern-
ment subscription and Bangladesh Bank net profit appropriation (the two
funds totaled Tk 27.5 million as of June 30, 1974), but the operating rules
and procedures governing the guarantee system have yet to be worked out and
enforced. At present, these funds are being operated in an ad-hoc manner.
In actual fact, they have been used mainly for a refinancing or conversion
loan to BJSB, rather than to guarantee casual losses. Sonali Bank, however,
has been guaranteed up to 30% of the amount lent.
36.       While the overall improvement of the operation of guarantee fund
must be tackled at the national level, two measures will be taken to safe-
guard the rural credit component of this project:
(a) The Sonali Bank will continue to receive a guarantee up
to 30% of the amount it lends under the project.
(b)  For medium-term credit, the 1.25% of the interest allocated
will be set aside and deposited to the Bangladesh Bank as
contribution to the Guarantee Fund. For short-term credit,
3.25% will be allocated to the Bangladesh Bank as a
contribution to the Guarantee Fund.
37.       By strictly enforcing the loan eligibility, the above provisions
would provide sufficient guarantee to rural credit extended under this
project under normal conditions.
-95-



ANNEX 6
Page 14
Provisions for Natural Calamities
38.       Bangladesh farms are subject to flood hazards.  In the past those
with crops destroyed by the flood could not repay the loan on the due date.
For many years, the Government has provided taccavi loans to calamity
victims. The current procedure is for the Union Council to draw up a
list of farmers' names and their crop area damaged.  The taccavi loan is
then disbursed by the Ministry of Agriculture according to the lists. For
the lack of means to verify whose crop is actually damaged, the loans go
mostly to larger farmers who are able to get their names on the Union
Council list. These loans are seldom repaid. The calamities also give
farmers excuses to default on their regular rural credit borrowed from
BJSB and other banks, even if their crops were not damaged.
39.       Under the project, the TCCA-KSS land register and maps will make
field checking of the area of calamity struck TCCA-KSS land possible. For
those whose crops are actually destroyed, the repayment of their rural credit
will not be written off, but rescheduled.
Improvement of Loan Records and Documents
40.       Improvements to be made are given in Exhibit 2 of this Annex.
April 1976

BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Proposed Procedure of Recovery of Overdue
Cooperative Credit in Project Thanas
1.        The overdues recovery would imply 4 stages:
A.   Inventory of overdues.
B.   Staff personal contracts with defaulters.
C.   Rescheduling overdue balances.
D.   Legal action against chronic defaulters.
2.        While Stage 1 would be in process, Stage 2 would be actively
engaged.  Stage 3 would be carried out as soon as Stage 1 is achieved and
Stage 4 would be the ultimate sanction in case of failure of Stages 2
and/or 3.
A. Inventory of Overdues
3.        Inventory of overdues would be made at the level of primary
cooperative societies (KSS or UMPCs). In absence of due datebook, data
would be extracted from the loan ledger in which borrowing member's
accounts are kept, usually showing:
-    type of loan (T. boro, aus/B. aman, T. aman ...),
-    borrowing members' name and address,
-    year during which the loan has been issued,
-    the amount of loan disbursed (debit entry),
-    the date the loan has to be repaid,
-    the date and amounts of partial or total repayments
(credit entries) of principal and interest,
-    the outstanding balance of principal and interest.
-97-



ANNEX 6
Exhibit 1
Page 2
4.        Some primary cooperative societies, whose loan ledger does not
mention any due date, would have to get this essential data from the
corresponding production plan, from the loan agreement, or in some cases
from the loan notice.
5.        For each type of loan the overdues inventory would be made as
shown on the attached model 1, where overdue balances, in principal, are
classified due-date wise. Such statement would give for each type of loan
the detail and the total of overdues in principal and interest and the
overdues in principal classified by age.
6.        After completing the same statement for each type of loan, these
would be consolidated so as to give for each borrowing member the total of
overdues and age of these overdues. If, however, the principle that de-
faulters are not eligible for a new loan, has always been strictly complied
with, the overdue balance would comne from only one loan and consolidation
of borrowing member's overdues wouLd not be necessary.
7.        At the CCB/TCCA level, thae consolidated statement of overdues re-
ceived from primary cooperative societies would be checked with the Primary
Cooperative Society overdues shown by their own loan ledger and then aggre-
gated, P. Coop. Soc. wise, in a total CCB/TCCA overdue statment (see Model 2),
themselves consolidated at Dacca Head Office level.
8.        The overdues inventory at primary Cooperative Society level would
give, for each defaulter, informatLon on the rate of repayment within the
due date and the annual repayment effort made by the defaulter the ensuing
years.
B. Staff Personal Contacts with Defaulters
9.        Without waiting for the completion of Stage 1 a recovery campaign
would be launched in each CCB or TCCA lending area. One of the most frequent
causes of loan delinquency is the absence of personal contacts between bor-
rowers and the lender staff all along the credit life, and the lack of credit
education in the rural community. A borrower must know that he is under the
permanent supervision of CCB or TCCA staff and that these staff are well
aware of his farming capacity and -repayment ability. He must then be con-
vinced that if he can repay he must repay. If he does not, he must receive
a second notice.
10.       The CCB or TCCA management and staff, from the Chairman to the
Junior Clerk must actively be engaged in the loan recovery campaign. At the
primary Coop. society level everyone in the managing committee, the manager,
the accountant, the supervisors must visit defaulters if they do not attend
the weekly meeting. They have to ,onvince them that they have to repay their
overdues principal and interest. They have to repeat these visits increasing
-98-



ANNEX 6
Exhibit 1
Page 3
the pressure for repayment and finally making them clearly understand that
drastic action would follow soon if they persist in delaying repayment.
Such personal pressure would be preceded by clear reminders which would
be delivered by messenger or through members attending the weekly meeting.
These reminders would be repeated twice at 15 days interval with stronger
terms and if necessary the latest would fix a deadline within which P. Coop.
Soc. representative would visit the defaulter and attempt to recover the
overdues. Such insistence would lead some defaulters to repay their arrears
and some others to negotiate easy term repayments. The latter would be
eligible for loan rescheduling. As to those who would show no willingness
to honor their debts, legal action should be taken immediately.
C. Rescheduling Overdue Balances
11.       Rescheduling overdue balances would be the responsibility of CCB
or TCCA depending on who had financed the initial loan. The CCB would
establish a rescheduling committee of three members consisting of the Chair-
man, the executive officer and the deputy executive officer and in the case
of TCCA, the manager, accountant and the IRDP/TPO. The Chairman and the
manager of the Primary Coop. Society or KSS involved in the rescheduling
would be a consultative members of this Committee.
12.       The period over which the overdue balances should be rescheduled
would be determined by the defaulter's annual capacity of repayment. Until
borrower's files contain proper and reliable data on annual farming plan,
farming cost, crops yield and crop sales proceeds, the defaulter's capacity
of repayment will be difficult to estimate and the rescheduling period would
have to be fixed arbitrarily, taking into account however, the amount to
be rescheduled and the defaulter's cultivable acreage. The following period
could be fixed:
Overdue Balance                Cultivate Acreage          Rescheduling Terms
less than 300 Takas            less than 1 ac                    4 years
1 to 3 ac                        3 years
more than 3 ac                    2 years
300 to 500 Takas               less than 2 ac                    4 years
2 to 4 ac                        3 years
more than 4 ac                    2 years
over 500 Takas                 less than 3 ac                    4 years
3 to 5 ac                        3 years
more than 5 ac                    2 years
-99-



ANNEX 6
Exhibit I
Page 4
13.       Rescheduling would apply to overdues in both principal and in-
terest. The present rate of interest would be applied to the rescheduling
operation. The rescheduling agreeTment would clearly stipulate and the de-
faulter should clearly understand that failing to repay the yearly install-
ments when due, despite a reminder sent 30 days before the due date, would
cause the cancellation of the rescheduling agreement and would, without warn-
ing, give rise to legal action for the recovery of the whole amount still due
in principle plus interest including a penalty of 10% per annum calculated
on the outstanding balance with effect from the last repayment date.
D. L,egal Action
14.       Proceedings for overdues recovery would be engaged against
defaulters who despite reminders and BJSB/CCB/TCCA/Sonali Bank staff personal
pressure did not show any willingness to repay their debts.
15.       Such proceedings would be. the responsibility of a legal action
department which would be established at BJSB and IRDP, Dacca head office
each headed by experienced lawyers and staffed with young and dynamic clerks.
16.       This department would keep records so as to follow up the progress
of the proceedings and to keep them going at a fast pace, up to the final
conclusion.
17.       Monthly reports to BJSB and IRDP/Sonali Bank management would show
the status of legal actions in progress and their expected final results.

BiNGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Traditional Cooperatives & TCCAs
Introduction
1.        There are two types of' agricultural cooperatives in Bangladesh--
the traditional cooperatives anc. the Comilla type of new cooperatives, now
called Thana Central Cooperative Associations (TCCA). The traditional
cooperatives are supervised by the Department of Cooperatives headed by the
Registrar of Cooperative Societies. The TCCAs are supervised by a relatively
new agency, the Integrated Rural Development Program (IRDP) headed by a
Director General. Both agencies are under the Ministry of Local Government
Rural Development and Cooperatives. The project has chosen TCCA rather
than the traditional cooperatives as the rural cooperative system for
channeling farm inputs, rural credit and technical agricultural services,
because the TCCA, though having shortcomings of its own, is much more
viable and better staffed than the traditional cooperatives. This is con-
sistent with the Government's Rural Development Strategy as outlined in
Bangladesh's first Five-Year Plan promulgated in 1973.
Traditional Cooperatives
2.        The traditional cooperatives which have never been successful
started as early as 1904 in the Indian subcontinent, mainly as village
agricultural credit societies. rhe partition of the subcontinent in 1948
caused collapse of cooperative banks, particularly in Punjab and Bengal
where mass relocation of population occurred. This was followed by mass
liquidation of village credit societies in Bengal.
3.        Under Pakistan, the defunct credit societies were reorganized into
Union Multi-purpose Cooperative Societies (UMCPS), one per union in principle.
Today, there are about 4,200 UMC7?S in 4,500 unions of Bangladesh, with a
total membership of about 1.3 miLlion.  Although UMPCSs are supposed to
serve the unions, their membershLp is often confined to a few villages around
those of the chairmen and secretaries. Their functions are hardly multi-
purpose, but still confined to providing credit and they have poor recovery
records. Defaulters are mostly Larger farmers (Annex 6).
4.        Since introduction of 'Low-lift pumps in 1968/69, an increasing num-
ber of pump irrigation societies (TIP-KSS) were established. Today, there
are about 25,000 TIP-KSS 1/ affi:Liated to the traditional cooperative system.
1/   There are about 15,000 other pump societies belonging to TCCAs.    -106-



ANNEX 7
Page 2
The membership overlaps a great deal with UMPCS. Both are members of the
Central Cooperative Banks (CCB). There are 62 CCBs, one in each of the
62 subdivisions. Both UMPCS and TIP-KSS are mainly controlled by larger
farmers in the community. At the thana level, there is a Thana Cooperative
Officer whose responsibility it is to supervise the traditional cooperatives.
There has been minimal supervision of cooperatives by the Thana Cooperative
Officersand the Central Cooperative Banks. The numbers of UMPCS and TIP-KSS
in the seven thanas are shown in Exhibit 1 of this Annex.
5.        Acting as the financial arm of the Cooperative Department at the
apex is the Bangladesh Jatiya Samabaya Bank (BJSB), the former East Pakistan
Provincial Cooperative Bank. The BJSB finances three main systems of
traditional cooperatives of which the CCB/UMPCS/TIP-KSS system mentioned
above is the largest. The other two are the Central Sugarcane Societies
with some 500 primary societies and about 63,000 members, and the Fishermen
Cooperatives, with some 2,000 primary societies and about 250,000 members.
6.        Management of BJSB consists of a Management Committee, members
of which are partly Government officials and partly elected by the share-
holders. Until recently, the Registrar of Cooperative Societies, who is
the Chief Executive Officer of the Department of Cooperatives, served as
Chairman of the Committee. The Chairman, now elected, is assisted by an
officer deputied by the Bangladesh Bank and some 40 staff members, most of
whom have only limited experience in Banking. The BJSB has no branches,
but channels funds directly to Central Cooperative Banks (CCB). The BJSB
is therefore not a bank in a real sense, but rather a quasi-Government
committee that makes proposals to and receives funds from the Government
for financing rural credit through the above-mentioned three channels.
It possesses little means of effectively supervising the credit operations
by its member banks. As of March 31, 1974, total outstanding loans disbursed
by BJSB were about Tk 400 million, of which Tk 257 million were overdue. 1/
7.        The traditional cooperative system is thus intertwined with the
cooperative banking system. Both being ineffective, one cannot be cured
without simultaneously curing the other. Strengthening of either will
entail major reorganization with national ramifications which cannot be
accomplished within a short period of time. Discussions have been going on
for some time within the Government on what to do with both systems, but no
decisions have been reached. The only way that the Rural Development
Project could proceed in the project thanas is therefore to channel its
activities through the more viable parallel cooperative system, the
TCCAs.
1/   Agricultural Credit Performance (1974-75), by Agricultural Credit
Department, Bangladesh Bank, June, 1974.
-107-



ANNEX 7
Page 3
Thana Central Cooperative Associations (TCCAs)
8.        The TCCA cooperaLive system is based on the model developed at
the Bangladesh Academy for Rural Development in Comilla. The Government
adopted a national rural development plan in 1970, which provided for the
adoption of the Comilla model throughout the country in a phased manner.
It also created a central agency, the Integrated Rural Development Program
(IRDP) in the same year to supervLse its implementation.  Some one hundred-fifty
(150) TCCAs were in existence as of November, 1974 and IRDP has been given
a goal to establish 250 by June, :1976.
9.        The TCCA adopts a two-tt.er system.  The TCCA headquarters is
located at the thana headquarters. in the same compound as the Thana Train-
ing and Development Center (TTDC) where the Circle Officers (chief civil
servants at the thana level, one for development and one for revenue) and
all other line department officers are located.  It forms primary societies
(KSS) 1/ at the village level. Whlile there is only one TCCA in each thana,
the number of KSS under the TCCA varies with the number of villages in the
thana.
10.       Both TCCA and its KSS ate managed by an elected Management Committee.
Each KSS elects a chairman, an accountant and a model farmer. The following
are unique features of the Comilla model of cooperatives.
(a) The chairman, accountant and model farmer of each KSS attend
weekly training courses at TTDC; chairman and accountants are
trained by the IRDP Thana project officer and model farmers
by the Thana Agricultural Officer, Livestock Officer, and BADC
officers (Annex 18). While in TTDC for training, they also
attend to matters concerning supplies and credit for KSS members.
(b) Weekly member meetings are held, where the chairman, accountant
and model farmer would explain to members what they learned at
the TTDC; KSS farm plans would be discussed; a one-taka contri-
bution to share capital by each member (Tk 52 per year) and
thrift saving deposits would be collected. Accumulation of
share capital and savings accounts provide some measure of
guarantee to rural credit borrowed by KSS members from banks.
(c) TCCA hires field inspect:rs and accountants who visit KSS
regularly. The standard pattern is to have an assistant
inspector per 12 KSS, a village accountant per six KSS,
one inspector for each five assistant inspectors and one
1/   The TCCA-KSS (Village Coopera-ive Society of TCCA, the Comilla type
of cooperative) is not to be confursed with TIP-KSS (para 4) which
are pump groups belonging to t:he traditional cooperative system.
-108-



ANNEX 7
Page 4
chief inspector. The frequent contact with KSS by
inspectors and village accountants was one contributing
factor to a good rural credit repayment record in the
prime years of the Comilla experience.
(d) TCCA transmits requests for inputs and rural credit from
KSS to agencies concerned and channels the same to farmers.
11.       The IRDP has three officers at the thana level:  A Project Officer
(college graduate); a Deputy Project Officer, and an Accountant. The Pro-
ject Officer serves as the secretary of the TCCA Management Committee.
Between thana and headquarters, IRDP posts Regional and District Project
Officers. The TCCA system is therefore much better structured, staffed
and supervised than the traditional cooperatives. The number of TCCA-KSS
in the seven project thanas are given in Exhibit 1.
12.       Until 1973, TCCAs have been receiving short-term credit from BJSB
or CCBs. Beginning with the T. aman crop season of 1973, the Sonali Bank
(the largest commercial bank in Bangladesh, now nationalized) took over the
short-term financing of the TCCAs. Since then, TCCA has become completely
independent from the traditional cooperative system, except that the regis-
tration of new TCCA-KSS is still the responsibility of the Thana Cooperative
Officer.
Shortcoming of TCCAs
13.       Although the TCCA system is a much healthier institution than the
traditional cooperatives, it is structurally deteriorating. Unless the
deterioration is arrested, it may slip into the rut of the traditional
cooperatives:
(a) While receiving rural credit from BJSB and CCBs, many TCCA-KSS
members also began to default on repayment. Since the Sonali
Bank took over the short-term credit financing, the repayment
record since then has been much improved (Annex 6). The
pressure on TCCAs to follow the bad examples of the traditional
cooperatives which got away with repeated defaulting is great,
and must be resisted by all means.
(b) The Management Committees of TCCAs and KSSs are also
dominated by larger farmers, and there has been criticism
of their manipulating the fertilizer distribution. It is
only because of the stronger supervision of IRDP officers
that their manipulation has not become as blatant as those
of the traditional cooperatives. The IRDP officers'
interference, however, has created friction in some TCCAs
between the general manager and the officers.
-109-



ANNEX 7
Page 5
(c) Because new extension material has not been supplied to
TAOs since liberation, and because of the general depres-
sion, the attendance of model farmer training has fallen,
even after the sessions were reduced from weekly to
bi-weekly. The attendance of weekly KSS member meetings
and weekly payment of KSS share capital have also become
lax. The farms of some model farmers in the seven project
thanas are no better than their neighbors.
(d) The TCCAs are supposed to pay the inspectors and village
accountants out of their own resources as soon as they
are financially able to do so. Thus far, these officers
in the seven project thanas are still being paid out of
an IRDP subsidy fund, because of TCCA's lack of income.
(e) The TCCA-KSS membership register provides no information
on members assets (such as land and livestock), nor
liabilities. The KSS farm plan, on which rural credit
application is based, has to depend on information
provided by applicants themselves, casual-checking among
farmers at the weekly meetings, and the knowledge of
the inspectors.
(f) The activities of TCCA-KSS in the seven project thanas,
which are generally only one to three years old, are
mainly confined to low-lift pump societies.  Their
membership covers less than 10% of the total farm
families and of the cultivated land area and 13% of
the prospective membership.
(g) The TCCA-KSSs so far have demonstrated little ability in
cooperative processing and marketing activities.
14.       The TCCA, therefore, is a cooperative system with a healthy concept,
well suited to the social structure of rural Bangladesh and with a record of
success during the Comilla days, but it is now at a crossroads of either
going downhill to join the traditional cooperatives, or being revived to
become a truly viable rural institution of Bangladesh. Since the traditional
cooperatives are in a much worse condition, there is little choice but to
help make TCCA a success. To expand and strengthen TCCA is the chosen strategy
of the Government for rural development. But increasing its number too rapid-
ly in too many thanas may produce negative results by stretching the available
manpower of IRDP too thinly. The actual need is to reestablish improved pro-
cedures for all TCCA-KSS activities, reestablish discipline, to revive and
sustain the training program, and to make TCCA-KSS financially viable.
-110-



ANNEX 7
Page 6
Project Approach
15.       Basically, the project will first reinstitute in the seven thanas
the proven practices adopted in the Comilla system in the prime years as
outlined in para 10 above. It will then bring further improvement to the
Comilla system. All inputs (low-lift pumps, shallow tubewells, hand pumps,
simple processing equipment) and rural credit provided by the project will
be distributed exclusively to TCCA-KSS members. All existing TIP-KSS pump
groups would be reorganized into TCCA-KSS by due processes. All technical
supporting services (extension, veterinarian, fishery) to TCCA-KSS will be
greatly strengthened. Rehabilitated publicly-owned fish ponds will be granted
to TCCA fishery Cooperatives composed of landless people in TCCA-KSS villages.
The TCCA-KSS members will be assured to receive fertilizer at official prices.
The above benefits are designed to make joining TCCA-KSS highly attractive
to small farmers. Rapid increase of coverage of agricultural households by
TCCA is projected.
16.       On the other hand, discipline in TCCA-KSS operation and management
will be significantly tightened, particularly with regard to the repayment
of rural credit. Specific actions designed to rectify the present short-
comings of TCCA are given in the following paragraphs.
A.  To Revitalize Training Sessions of KSS Managers, Accountants, and Model
Farmers
17.       The IDA Agricultural and Rural Training Project includes building
inter alia, four Zonal Cooperative Institutions. Swedish Aid is expected to
provide experts to assist IRDP in training its officers. New extension
material will be prepared and TAOs/UAAs retrained under this project in
coordination with the Agricultural and Rural Training project and UNDP/FAO
Extension project (Annex 10). The German Livestock Team is expected to
assist the Livestock Department in training Thana Livestock Officers and
Assistants in the seven thanas. The Fishery Department experts of the
North Bengal Water Development Project would assist the Thana Fishery Officers
in conducting demonstrations of pond management. While bilateral and inter-
national experts will upgrade the officers, the latter will help to revitalize
the weekly training sessions of the TCCA-KSS managers, accountants and model
farmers under this project. The training sessions will be synchronized with
crop seasons, so that the topics will dwell on works to be done by KSS members
in the following week or following month. A list of training topics is given
in Exhibit 1 of Annex 18.
B. To Improve the Information Base of KSS Membership
18.       The IRDP will undertake a basic improvement of the KSS membership
register to provide information on KSS members' assets (land and livestock
and liabilities:
-111-



ANNEX 7
Page 7
(a)  Local land surveyors (Amins) 1/ will be employed under
the project if necessary to assist TCCA-KSS managers to
prepare a register of land parcels owned by each KSS
member and an accompanying village mouja map 2/ showing
members' land parcels. The KSS manager and model farmer
will, through weekly memlber meetings and interviews, label
each members' land parcels by suitability for Aus, T. aman,
boro, jute and other crops by registering their present use.
(b)  From (a), lists will be prepared to indicate KSS
member-owned land suitabLe for aus, T. aman,
and boro, jute and other crops.
(c)  The improved information will be used for:
(i)  preparation of seasonal production plan,
checking applications for seeds, fertilizers,
low-lift pumps, shaLlow tubewells, and rural
credit by members;
(ii) planning of extension activities;
(iii) facilitating field verification of area of HYV
planted, irrigated by pumps and wells, reported
damage by flood, etc.; and
(iv) formulating sound KSS development plan including
production, processing marketable volume and
employment of landless villagers.
(d)  The work will be started by IRDP by trial operation in
a few KSSs. Procedures will be established, training will
take place, and then full operation will begin.
1/   Amins are local land surveyors, a profession created during the days of
Indian Survey which produced the "mouja maps" and cadastral records in
the early 1900s, and since handed down in the families. They are availa-
ble in all rural areas. Today, they are hired by rural and urban people
in land transactions. They can survey, prepare maps and set land title
registration record straight with the Thana Revenue Office.
2/   Mouja maps are old cadastral maps prepared by the Surveyor General's
Department of India in pre-independence days. They have a scale of
16 inches to one mile, and show boundaries of each land parcel at the
time of the survey. Although the original parcels have been sub-
divided, the mouja maps are still the basis of the land registry
records which are kept in the Thana Revenue Offices.
-112-



ANNEX 7
Page 8
19.       Each KSS will also prepare a register of the draft animals owned
by its members and keep the register current. The register will be used for
arranging mass vaccination and deworming of animals by the Thana Vaccinators
(Annex 9).
20.       The project will also carry out an inventory of outstanding debts
of TCCA-KSS, and require rescheduling of repayment of outstanding loans
before they are eligible to further loans, and the same for TIP-KSS and
UMPCS members before they are eligible to TCCA-KSS membership.
C. To Assure Small Farmers Receiving Fair Share of Inputs and Credit
21.       Based on the improved information concerning each members' assets
and liabilities, KSS will prepare seasonal production plans (aus, T. aman,
boro, jute and other crops), members' application for fertilizers, pesticides,
and short-term credit (when applicable). Individual member's application data
would be verifiable in the office (against registry) and in the field (against
maps).
22.       The KSS fertilizer application will be channeled through TCCA/IRDP
to BADC for allocation of fertilizers. The KSS will take bulk delivery of
fertilizers and distribute to member farmers according to the KSS production
plan for that season. Cost of fertilizers covered by the rural credit (from
the Sonali Bank) for each KSS will be paid directly by TCCA to BADC (Exhibit
2). The TCCA-KSS would take delivery from BADC or thana godowns, instead of
buying from appointed dealers.
23.       Procedures for application of low-lift pumps and shallow tubewells
from BADC are described in Annex 4, those for application of medium-term
credit (shallow tubewells, hand pumps, paddy husking machines and small
paddy driers), in Annex 6.
24.       Applications based on improved KSS member registers will be pre-
pared in weekly member meetings, instead of being prepared by the Manage-
ment Committee. Each small farmer will be made full aware of his share
of inputs and credit and that he must plant according to area and variety
as he stated in the KSS production plan and fertilizer/rural credit
applications.
25.       The KSS weekly member meetings will be made important working
sessions with all members, large and small farmers, fully participating in
economic and financial activities of their cooperatives. The IRDP Thana
Officers, Thana Extension Officers, TCCA Inspectors and Village Accountants
who visit KSS on scheduled days will check and make sure that important
messages have reached small farmers, and that they understand their rights
and responsibilities.
-113-



ANNEX 7
Page 9
D. Expansion of TCCA-KSS Membership2
26.       The TCCA-KSS membership is expected to increase yearly until a 100%
coverage of the "prospective households" (150,000 in seven thanas) is reached
ten years after commencing project implementation. The "'prospective house-
holds" equal to 55% of the total 1974 households of the seven thanas and 83%
of the farming households are illustrated below:
(a)  Total Rural Households in Seven Thanas               275,000 1
(b)  Trade, processing services (9% of (a)) 2/             24,750
(c)  Landless, non-share-cropper (25% of (a)) 2/           68,750
(d)  Farming Households                                   181,500

(10% of (d)) 3/                                     18,150
(g)  Unwilling to join (2% of (d)) 3/                       3,630
(h)  Total Prospective TCCA-KSS Households                150,000 (rounded)
27.       In the first year of the project, IRDP/TCCA together with the
cooperative office will concentrate efforts in reorganizing the non-TCCA-
TIP/KSS into TCCA-KSS pump groups. Beginning the second project year,
IRDP/TCCA will extend the organization of new KSSs into rainfed aus and
T. aman areas, reorganize UMPCSs into TCCA-KSSs and recruit new members
into the existing KSS. The projected rate of increase of KSS and membership
are given in Exhibit 3 of this Annex.
28.       After the Thana Fishery Officers have surveyed and located the
public fish ponds in the seven thanas on the mouja maps, IRDP/TCCA will
organize the landless households in the nearby villages into TCCA Fishery
cooperatives (Annex 10), involving approximately 5,000 families for managing
500 ac of fish ponds as a source of supplementary income.
E.  To Strengthen Technical SupportLng Services to TCCA-KSS Members
29.       Specific steps for streng:hening the extension service to TCCA-KSS
members in the seven thanas are presented in Annex 8, those for livestock
services, in Annex 9, and those for Fishery Services, in Annex 10.

30.       Special attention will be given by IRDP to facilitate the accep-
tance of the technical departments' advice by TCCA-KSS members. Under the
project, the thana IRDP/TCCA officers would:
(a) cooperate with technical officers in formulating the
weekly training sessions for KSS managers, accountants,
and model farmers, but leave the technical advice
entirely to the technical officers;
(b) ensure full attendance of the training sessions;
(c) arrange with the technical officers for the latter's
scheduled visits to KSSs, and notify the KSS of the same,
so that not only large farmers, but also small farmers
would be on hand to take advantage of the officers' visits; and
(d) follow-up technical officers' advice in KSS members' weekly
meeting for wide acceptance and provide feedback to technical
officers;
F. To Increase IRDP/TCCA Staff in the Seven Thanas
31.       In view of the greatly increased work load and the projected in-
crease in number of KSSs and membership (Exhibit 3), one new Deputy Thana
Project Officer and a full-time Thana Training Officer will be added to
the existing three-man staff (para 11) of each thana.
32.       The total number of inspectors, assistant inspectors and village
accountants in seven TCCAs will increase as follows:

C.   To Improve the Financial Position of TCCA-KSS
33.       The above projection is based on the standing ratios between
number of officers and number of KSS established by the TCCA. It is expected
that under the project, the increased cost of these TCCA officers will be
paid by the TCCAs. At the end of the first and second years, IRDP would
review the financial position of the TCCAs, to determine what proportions
of the salary, training cost, trave:l and operation and maintenance of vehicles
can be met by TCCA's income from hanldling the rural credit, fertilizers and
performing other services. The IRDP would grant funds to the TCCAs only
in amounts necessary to meet deficii:s. It is expected that the TCCAs will
be financially self-sufficient by the third year of project implementation.
34.       The KSS will use the income from increased rural credit commission
strictly for administration, collection commission and, provision for bad
debt as specified in Annex 6, para. 30.
35.       The IRDP/Cooperative Department will audit the TCCA-KSS
accounts according to standard procedures.

BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Fertilizer Allocation for TCCA - KSS
Members in Project Thanas
To help strengthen TCCA as a basic farmers' organization of
Bangladesh, a system for allocation of fertilizers to TCCA-KSS members will
be adopted in the project thanas, as follows:
(a)  For each crop season (aus, T. aman, boro), each TCCA-KSS
will prepare a farm plan, based on the improved KSS
membership land register and KSS land maps to be intro-
duced by IRDP. The seasonal farm plan will list area of
rice (separately for HYV and local varieties) and other
crops to be grown by each member, and the KSS total. The
farm plan will be prepared during the weekly member-meetings
of KSS well in advance oi the planting time of that season.
(b)  In the same member meetings, KSS members will be told of the
allocations of urea: TSP and MP per ac of HYV rice, local
variety rice, and other crops, and the official prices per
maund (or seer) of different fertilizer. Members will be
informed about the quantity of fertilizers each is entitled
to receive.
(c) Rural credit application will be processed during the same
meetings; a subtotal will be made in the plan for credit
covering the cost of fertilizers.
(d)  Based on the farm plan, three sets of totals will be
obtained by each KSS:
(i) total areas of HYV rice, local variety rice
and other crops to be grown by KSS members
during that season;
(ii) total quantity of fertilizers requested by
the KSS; and
(iii)  total rural credit requirement of the KSS for that
season, with a sub-total for fertilizer credit.
-120-



ANNEX 7
Exhibit 2
Page 2
(e) The KSS chairman will submit the farm plan with the above
totals to TCCA for transmittal to the Thana Project Executive
Committee. On behalf of the Committee, the Thana Inspector,
BADC, the TEO/TAO and IRDP Thana Project Officer will
verify the soundness of the farm plan with regard to the
crop acreages reported. This can be done quickly by
checking against the KSS land - register (a copy of which
will be in the IRDP Thana Project Officer's file) to see
if the total rice area is in line with the land registers
figures for that season (HYV area planted will be verified
during the crop growing season. The IRDP/TPO will complete
thana totals, and submit them to the Thana Inspector, BADC,
with copies to the Sonali Bank branch office.
(f) When fertilizers arrive at the thana godown (BADC or TCCA),
each TCCA-KSS will take bulk delivery from the godown for
all members and distribute to members according to the
original farm plan. Rural credit for fertilizers for each
KSS will be issued by the Sonali Bank by a check in favor
of BADC, and this check will be transmitted by Sonali Bank
to Thana Inspector, BADC upon receipt of the fertilizers
by the KSS.
(g) As to the areas planted to HYV rice, in the first place,
the KSS (assisted by IRDP Officers) will consult the
TEO/TAO in formulating its HYV extension target for each
season. Secondly, TEO/TAO/UAAs will verify the area
planted by spot - check in the field when they make
scheduled visits to the KSSs (see Annex 8). Since the
growing season in the main field is at least three
months, the spot check can be made on any day during
this period. The work will be made easy by the use of
the KSS land maps.

BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Agriculi:ural Extension
Present Deficiencies
1.        There are too few extension officers in Bangladesh and they are
insufficiently trained. Also, they have not been supplied with comprehensive
and up-to-date extension material, and they are immobile for lack of vehicles.
In the seven project thanas, there are seven Thana Agricultural Officers (TAOs),
one for each thana, 90 Union Agricultural Assistants (UAAs), one for each
union, 470,000 ac of cultivated land and about 250,000 agricultural families,
averaging about 5,200 ac or 2,800 rural families per UAA. The UAAs are the
only officers at the union level. Since they are asked to do many non-agri-
cultural odd jobs and paper work they have little time to do field extension
work.
2.        Most UAAs have neither living quarters or offices; some get room
and board free in larger farmers' houses, and thus are influenced in allo-
cation of pumps, fertilizers, etc.
3.        Since 1970, little new extension information has reached Thana
Agricultural Officers (TAO) and UAAs. They are still using old material
for weekly training of model farmers. This has caused the attendance of
model farmers to decline significantly even though the sessions have been
reduced from weekly to bi-weekly.
4.        The TAOs and UAAs have the responsibility of:  reporting crop
statistics; HYV acreages vs extension targets; flood damaged areas; help-
ing the union council in drawing up lists of fertilizer recipients based
on HYV area planted as well as recipients of taccavi loans based on damaged
cropped area, and checking pump irrigated area in boro season for the Thana
Irrigation Committee, etc. But t-hey are not provided with vehicles, and
don't really have usable data for verifying such acreages.
5.        A combination of the above factors contributes to the ineffective-
ness of the extension service. Particularly detrimental has been the frequent
involvement of the extension staff in food procurement activities which has
prevented them from gaining the trust and confJdence or the farmers. The
TAOs/UAAs are not to be blamed. They are the victims of he!nF overloaded
with important responsibilities without being given means to do their
jobs.
-124-



ANNEX 8
Page 2
Project Approach
6.        In the seven project thanas, the number of extension staff will
be increased; vehicles would be provided to make the staff mobile and the
extension staff would be asked to concentrate on extension activities.
The UAAs will have living and office quarters; training and extension
material will be made current, and the officers will be retrained. They
will make scheduled visits to TCCA-KSS model farmers. Their field work will
be facilitated by an improved TCCA-KSS membership register and KSS land maps
to be prepared by IRDP/TCCA. Specific steps are presented in Exhibit 1 to
this Annex entitled "Establishment of Intensified Extension Service in Seven
Rural Development Project Thanas". The contents of the Exhibit have been
agreed upon by the Government.
7.        Two aspects'regarding the training of model farmers by TEO/TAO are
steps forward from the Comilla tradition:
(a) The renting of a training farm close to each TTDC and
introduction of field practices by model farmers in the
bi-weekly training session, in addition to the tradi-
tional classroom lecture and passing out of lesson sheets
(Exhibit 1, para 6).
(b) The introduction of scheduled visits to KSSs by the
extension officers, and bringing members other than
the model farmer to meet the officer at the model
farmers' farm on the "visit day", to make sure that
the model farmer has practiced what he has learned in
the training sessions, and that the knowledge is spread
to other KSS members during the weekly member meetings
(Exhibit 1, paras 3-5).
8.        The "Special Extension Items Adaptable to KSS-wide Adoption"
(Exhibit 1, paras 7 and 8) are introduced to:
(a)  enable the KSS farms to start the planting of both HYV
and local varieties with uniform and strong seedlings
that would revive and grow rapidly after transplanting,
minimize the effect of drought or submergence by rising
flood, achieve uniform heading, a higher response to
fertilizers and a higher yield;
(b)  minimize the risk of crop failures by proper selection
of land for planting HYV; and
(c)  expand HYV rapidly by multiplying their own seeds under
a KSS plan, before the national seed program becomes
widely effective.
-125-



ANNEX 8
Page 3
9.        The TEO/TAO will be upgraded by training on HYV rice technology
and special items for KSS adoption at BRRI, and UAA/VEA at Agricultural
Training Institutes (Exhibit 1, para 11) under the national program assisted
by the IDA Agricultural and Rural Training Project and the UNDP Extension
Project. At the end of each training session, the Department of Agriculture
(Management and Extension) and IRDP would give the trainees a one day
briefing on the following:
(a)  how to conduct the new model farmer training;
(b) use of the TCCA-KSS land register and land maps
for verification of HYV, irrigated and flood dam-
aged areas; and
(c) scheduled visits by extension officers to TCCA-KSS.
10.       An indicative number of extension officers in the seven thanas
to be trained each year would be as follows:

Page 4
11.       The KSS land register and land maps will enable the extension
officers to verify the areas of HYV planted, irrigated by pump/well dam-
aged by flood or drought. These data have been the basis for application
and granting of fertilizers, pumps/wells, rural credit and taccavi loans.
Wild claims of areas and officers' lack of means to verify them contribute
to the manipulation of inputs and credit by larger farmers. Under the pro-
ject, these data will be made verifiable for the TCCA-KSS land register. By
phased increase of KSS and membership coverage in the thanas, such data will
be built up at an even pace, with data of a larger and larger percentage of
farmers becoming verifiable. This will be a basic improvement, not only
for TCCA-KSS, but for the execution of the over-all Rural Development Proj-
ect.
12.       While the above program centers on rice, the rural infrastructure
built into this project would serve other crops as well. The extension
material prepared by the Intensive Jute Cultivation Scheme of the Ministry
of Agriculture would be used for training model farmers on jute cultivation
in the Bogra Thanas (Mymensingh is not covered by the Scheme). Varieties,
fertilizer application and pest control measures recommended by the Scheme
would be followed. Utilizing TCCA-KSS weekly meetings and the KSS land
register and land maps, the project would pay special attention to trans-
mitting the following recommendations of the Scheme to jute growing KSS
members:
(a) selection of land for capsularies and olitorius
varieties,
(b) proper time of sowing; and
(c) line sowing and proper spacing between plants.
Officers of the Jute Extension Scheme would be invited to be the trainers
of the model farmers.
13.       Extension of vegetable growing would be conducted in conjunction
with the supply of hand pumps among the TCCA-KSS members.
Cost Estimates
14.       Motorcycles for TAOs and bicycles and living quarters for UAAs
have been provided under the IDA Agricultural and Rural Training Project, and
extension equipment has been provided by the UNDP Extension Project. The
Rural Development I Project would provide motorcycles to the seven TEOs and
bicycles to 180 VEAs who will be newly assigned to the seven thanas.
The cost estimates for the agricultural extension component totals
Tk 2.82 million (Table 1).

BANGLADESH
RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Establishment of Intensified Extension Service
The Department of Agriculture (Extension and Management) of the
Ministry of Agriculture will be responsible for implementing this project
component, in close cooperation with IRDP. As a part of Ministry of
Agriculture's effort to upgrade and intensify the agricultural extension
service to farmers, a field level working scheme will be adopted in the
seven project thanas. The scheme will fully utilize the TCCA-KSS infra-
structure for furthering the effectiveness of extension to small farmers.
Essential steps of the scheme are given below:
Increase of Numbers of Extension Officers at the Thana and Union Levels
1.        Realizing the present serious understaffing of field level exten-
sion officers, the Ministry of Agriculture has begun a sustained program
for training and employing increasing numbers of officers at the thana
and union levels. The plan is to create two new levels of officers: TEO
and VEA. The TEO would be better qualified than the existing TAO's and
VEA would supervise several villages. Thus far, 56 TEO's have been re-
cruited; and 226 VEA's are being trained (2-year course). Refresher courses
for TAO's and UAA's at ATI's and for ATI instructors at BERI are being con-
ducted.
2.        In these seven thanas, TEO's will be designated as soon as the
project starts to be implemented. The 90 unions in the seven thanas are
expected to be given prlority consideration for assigning Village Exten-
sion Agents (VIA's). The precise number to be assigned each year will be
worked out between the Department of Agriculture (Extension and Management)
and IRDP, based on the rate of organizing new KSS's by TCCA's in these seven
thanas. The VEAs employed in the project area shall be assigned only such
duties as are directly related to agricultural extension work.
Establishing "Scheduled Visit System" by TEO/TAO/UAA/VEA to TCCA-KSS's
3.        In these seven thanas, the extension officers will be provided
with vehicles by the IDA Rural Training Project and this project. The
TEO/TAO's in each thana will discuss with IRDP-TPO's and decide on a time
schedule for visiting KSS's on appointed dates. The TEO/TAO/UAA (and later
on, VEA's) will divide among themselves the work load of such scheduled visits.
129



ANNEX 8
Exhibit 1
Page 2
The KSS's will know on which day the officers will come. The visiting
extension officer will check whether the model farmers have adopted the
cultural practices taught at the training sessions. Other members will be
informed of the coming of the extension officer, and encouraged to join
the model farmer on his field. T'hey may bring any questions about the crop
in season to ask the officer. If the officer cannot answer on the spot,
he will find the answers and give them in the next training session of the
model farmers.
4.        On the "visit day", the TCCA inspector or assistant inspector
should also be present, so that he could assist the Chairman, the General
Manager and model farmer of the KS>S to further discuss the introduced
practices (in KSS weekly meetings) for wide adoption by members. On the
same "visits", the extension officer would also spot check the area planted
to HYV and other crops, pump irrigation area, etc. according to the KSS
farm plan and KSS land maps.
5.        During the first year of the project in the seven thanas, TEO/TAO
will participate in the scheduled visits to the existing TCCA-KSS, including
those reorganized from the existing non-TCCA TIP-KSS's. As new KSS's are
organized in the rainfed area of the thanas, the TEO/TAO will concentrate
on the new KSS's, while UAAs/VEAs will continue with the existing ones.
When even more KSS's are organized and improved practices have been estab-
lished as routine in the old KSS's, the TCCA inspectors and assistant
inspectors will carry on the routine follow-ups, but the UAA/VEA's will
visit KSS at least twice in a season. The IRDP-TPO and DTPO will make
their own scheduled visits to each KSS.
Field Practices for Model Farmer Training
6.        Under the project, a 1 ac training farm will be rented near the
TTDC in each of the seven thanas. The UNDP Extension Project Rice Produc-
tion Specialist will assist in planning of the curriculum and training
material for the bi-weekly training sessions for the model farmers which
will be syncronized with the crop season. The sessions will consist of
a short classroom explanation of the topic of the day by TEO or TAO, fol-
lowed by an action demonstration in the field by the farmer owner of the
rented farm (who had practiced prior to the session under the supervision
of the TEO/TAO), and followed, in turn, by model farmer trainees practicing
in the field. They don't need all to get into the field, but as many as
feasible will, and all of them would have seen how the work is to be done.
During the intervening two week period, the owner would manage the farm.
The HYV seeds, fertilizers, pesticides will be included as a part of the
rental. The owner will also retairn the entire harvested crop. The TAO/TEO
will supervise the owner farmer managing the training farm.
-130-



ANNEX 8
Exhibit 1
Page 3
Special Extension Items Adaptable to KSS-wide Adaption
7.        From the "Extension Package" information to be supplied by Depart-
ment of Extension through the normal channel, the following items will be
given additional emphasis for TCCA-KSS adoption:
A.   Preparation of good nurseries for T. aus, T. aman
and boro, proper way of handling seedlings and
proper depth of transplanting.
B.   As a part of good nursery management, nursery pest
control will be practiced.
C. How to keep farmers own seeds pure and viable, mul-
tiply their own seeds.
D.   Selection of land for T. aus HYV and T. aman HYV.
E.   Organized main field pest control.
8.        Similarly, from the Intensive Jute Cultivation Scheme's extension
package, selection of land for varieties, time of sowing, line sowing and
proper spacing would be introduced for KSS-wide adoption. Special emphasis
to these topics will be given in model farmer training and officer's "field
visits". The IRDP-TPO, DTPO and inspectors should also keep themselves
informed on these topics so they could assist the KSS manager and model
farmers in organizing KSS-wide adoption.
Coordination of International Assistance Projects in Fields of
Agricultural Extension, Information and Training
9.        Currently, several international/bilateral agencies are planning
projects in Bangladesh to assist the country in improving its agricultural
extension and training programs. Some of the lmportant projects are: UNDP
project for "strengthening the Agricultural Extension Service"; project from
the Government of Japan for the Central Extension Resources Institute; Ford
Foundation/IRRI project ar BRRI; UNDP/FAO Project on Land Use Advice to the
Ministry of Agriculture and the IDA Agricultural and Rural Training Project.
These projects will work with the various agencies of the Ministry of Agri-
culture such as BRRI, Department of Agriculture (Extension and Management)
Agricultural Information Service, Central Extension Resources Institute,
ATI's Bangladesh Soil Survey Service and the Accelerated Cereal Production
Program (ACPP). The National Committee on Rural Training, under the Ministry
of Planning will coordinate the work of these agencies and programs.

to the extension component of the project. The Ministry will assure that
all ministry agencies and other international/bilateral projects concerned
would render support to the project and cater to special needs of the
project. Agreements were reached between the Ministry and the international
projects concerned, for their expeets to provide technical assistance
to the project.

RURAL DEVELOPMENT I PROJECT
Livestock Improvement
Background
1.        The Department of Livestock reported 266,000 heads of draft
animals and 350,000 chickens in the seven thanas:
Thana                    Draft Animal              Chickens
head                    number
Bogra District
Kotwali                           46,360                  62,600
Sherpur                           22,120                 31,400
Gabtali                           47,440                 53,000
Sariakandi                        30,080                 60,800
Sub-total                        146,000                207,800
Mymensingh District
Muktagacha                        37,800                  43,400
Gaffargaon                        51,120                 58,200
Trisal                            30,560                  36,600
Sub-total                        119,480                 138,200
TOTAL                                 265,480                 346,000
2.        The above data on draft animals include adult males and females
at about 4:1 ratio, and average about one head per two cropped ac, or one
pair per four cropped ac. Data on calves is not available. The number of
chickens seems extremely low, averaging only two birds per land-owning
farm household. There is no way at present for the thana livestock
officers to obtain reliable statistics. More reliable information is
necessary for effective project implementation (para 12).
3.        The major cattle diseases in Bangladesh are hemorrhagic septi-
caemia, anthrax, black quarter, foot and mouth disease, liver fluke and
tuberculosis. The annual mortality rate due to all diseases is estimated
at about 8%. This would mean an annual death of about 21,000 head of
draft animals in the seven thanas. The foot and mouth disease does not
necessarily kill the infected animal, but puts about 20% of the animals
-137-



ANNEX 9
Page 2
out of work. In the seven thanas, this would amount to an annual work loss
of work of 53,000 head. The animals are further weakened by internal para-
sites which infest most of the herd.
4.        The major poultry diseases are new castle disease, fowl pox, fowl
cholera, coccidiosis, helminthiasis and deficiency diseases. The annual
mortality rate is not known, but it is believed to be very high. All chickens
are back yard scavangers fed on fallen grain, house scraps and other
natural food. Since the project area is deficient in human food, modern
poultry production based on grain feeding is unlikely in the near future.
5.        The seven thanas have at present 30 livestock officers with the
following titles:
Thana      Veterinarian  Veterinarian  Thana
Livestock  Assistani:       Field        Livestock  Insemi-
Thana       Officer    Surgeon         Assistant    Assistants  nator    Total
(TLO)       (VAS)          (VFA)           (TLA)

Cabtali         1            1             2               1         -          5

Trisal          1            1             1               -         -          2
Total      7             6            11               5         1         30
They obtain vaccine, serum and drugs from the Biological Product Laboratories
at Dacca and Comilla by submitting requisitions through the District Livestock
Officer. Tile supplies are taken by the DLO by rail, road or air, stored in
the District Officers' Office, anc distributed to thanas for use from time
to time. The thanas' veterinarian dispensaries do not have adequate
facilities for storing the supplies. Nor do the thana officers have
vehicles.
6.        Due to the shortage of supplies, the difficulty in moving supplies
and in visiting villages by the officers, the Department of Livestock reported
only about 10,000 head of cattle and 14,000 poultry birds as being vaccinated
in 1974 in the seven thanas. Vaccination is a free service in Bangladesh.
A Bogra District Artificial Insemination Center was established in 1974 and
a limited insemination has been administered in nearby thanas.
-138-



ANNEX 9
Page 3
7.        Draft animal feeds consist mainly of rice, straw, pulse crop
residues, sugarcane leaves, and grass along roadside and canal bunds
and on secondary growth in the paddy field after harvest. Feed becomes
low in the dry season and extremely low toward its end (April). The
animals are weakest when they are needed for preparing land for sowing
aus, broadcast aman and jute. Only small areas of fodder crops are grown.
A limited supply of fodder crops, rice bran, wheat bran, and oil cakes
are reserved for small commercial dairy herds and farmers' milk cattle.
No research or extension work on fodder or forage crops has been done
until recently. The Savar Farm (near Dacca) of the Livestock Department,
with assistance from a German livestock team, selected improved varieties
and initiated a field demonstration project in 1974.
Project Works
8.        As a part of an integrated rural development approach, the live-
stock component of this project would put major emphasis on lowering the
mortality and parasite infestation of the draft animals and chickens. This
would benefit small farmers by reducing the loss of animals and poultry.
The project would also popularize forage/fodder crops in the seven thanas.
9.        Equipment and Supplies:  The project would provide equipment
to the thana veterinarian dispensaries for safekeeping vaccine, serum,
drugs in thanas. Two motorcylces cabs would be provided for moving supplies
from the District stores to thana dispensaries. Motorcyles would be provided
to thana officers and bicycles to vaccinators. Vaccines, serum and drugs
would be supplied by the Livestock Department. Equipment and supplies are
listed in Table 1.
10.       Increase of Vaccinators:   A total of 70 vaccinators (average ten
per thana) would be recuited by the Livestock Department and deployed in the
project area over a five year period, at the rate of 14 vaccinators a year.
The new recruits plus the existing 12 VFAs and TLAs (para 5) would make
roughly one vaccinator per union. One additional TLA with a background in
agronomy would be assigned to each of the seven thanas for conducting field
demonstration of forage and fodder crops. The VAS would manage the refurnished
thana dispensary and daily dispatching of vaccinators. The TLO would administer
the thana program and training of vaccinators, model farmers and RSS vaccinators
(para 11).
11.       Scheduled Visits to TCCA-KSS by Livestock Officers:  The TLO,
IRDP/TPO and TCCA Manager would jointly decide time schedules and routes for
the vaccinators to visit each TCCA-KSS in the thana. On the scheduled day,
the KSS manager and model farmer would ask all farmers to keep their draft
animals in the village compound and chickens in coops, so that the vaccinator
could finish his work quickly and move on to the next village.
-139-



ANNEX 9
Page 4
12.       TCCA-KSS Membership Livestock Registration:  With assistance from
IRDP thana officers, TCCA-KSS would compile a livestock register, which
would record heads of adult and young draft animals owned by its members.
The register would be kept up-to-date by members reporting changes in the
weekly member meetings. Numbers vaccinated would also be recorded. Chickens
would not be registered, but the number to be vaccinated would be discussed
in the weekly member meeting prior to the "visit date" of the vaccinator.
The vaccination request would be sent to the Thana Livestock Officer when
the KSS officers go to TTDC for their weekly training sessions.
13.       Training of KSS Vaccinato_rs: As the number and membership of KSS
expands, the LFAs would have to vaccinate increasing numbers of animals each
year. A training program for KSS vaccinators would be launched to train
young KSS members to become vaccinators. The LFAs would bring sterilized
field equipment and supplies to the village on the "visit day" and move on
to other villages. The KSS vaccinator would vaccinate animals and poultry
for KSS members. This would enable the LFAs recruited during the five year
project implementation period to cope with the increasing numbers of animals
in years beyond that period.
14.       Projection of Vaccination Coverage:  About 55,000 head of draft
animals and 76,000 birds would be vaccinated in the first year of the project,
increasing to about 150,000 and 200,000 respectively by the fifth year. If
the KSS vaccinator training program is successful, the project is expected to
fully cover the draft animals and chickens belonging to KSS members in all
villages.
15.       Demonstration of Forage and Fodder Crops:  Based on experimental
results obtained at the Savar Farm, 12-24 demonstration fields would be set
up in each of the seven thanas. Each field would consist of three to five
plots: one or two for permanent fodder cropping (Napier and /or para
grasses), two to three for short season forage crops (sorghum, maize,
oats, Berseem clover, and sun flower). Each plot would be of 0.01 ac. The
combination of crops to be grown in each demonstration field would be deter-
mined by the land type and the present cropping pattern. Fodder crop plots
would be placed only on land where permanent cropping is feasible. Forage
crops would be tried out (a) in rab: season following mixed aus and deep-
water aman, (b) following aus/jute and transplanted aman and (c) in kharif
season following aus on the Barind Tract. The forage demonstration would
be conducted under both rainfed and irrigated conditions. It is expected
that the land to be irrigated by the 2,500 shallow tubewells and 5,000 hand
pumps to be provided by the project would foster diversification of rabi
crops, and improve the possibility of introducing some forage crops into the
crop rotation system. Successful demonstration and subsequent extension
would help to lessen the serious feed shortage during the dry season.
16.       The forage/fodder crop denonstration work would be conducted by
the Livestock Department so that it could be closely linked to the research
work being done at the Savar Farm and to the Department's own demonstration
-140-



ANNEX 9
Page 5
program in other thanas. When successful demonstration leads into extension
and seed multiplication, joint planning between the Department of Livestock,
Department of Agriculture (Extension and Management) and IRDP would be
necessary.
17.       Technical Assistance:  The German Livestock Team assisting the
Savar Farm is expected to provide technical assistance to the livestock
improvement component of the project.
18.       Plan for Collection of Fees:  Operation and maintenance costs of
laboratory and field equipment and vehicles would be provided during the
five year project implementation period. The Livestock Department and IRDP
would jointly launch a campaign 4mong TCCA-KSS members for collecting fees
for vaccination, after the program has produced a clear impact in the KSS
villages. The GOB would submit to IDA in the third year of the project a
plan for collecting such fees. The fees collected should be sufficient
for covering the operation, maintenance and replacement of the laboratory
and field equipment and vehicles provided by the project.
